# Antifragile — Detailed Notes

## Prologue

### I. How to Love the Wind

- **Wind as metaphor**: Wind extinguishes a candle and energizes fire — likewise with randomness, uncertainty, and chaos, you want to use them, not hide from them
- You want to be the fire and wish for the wind
- The goal is not merely to survive uncertainty but to **dominate, domesticate, and conquer** the unseen, the opaque, and the inexplicable — like a certain class of aggressive Roman Stoics who sought to have the last word

### II. The Antifragile

- **Core definition**: Some things benefit from shocks — they thrive and grow when exposed to volatility, randomness, disorder, stressors, and love adventure, risk, and uncertainty
- There is no word for the exact opposite of fragile — Taleb coins the term **antifragile**
- **Antifragility is beyond resilience or robustness** — the resilient resists shocks and stays the same; the antifragile gets better
- This property is behind everything that has changed with time: evolution, culture, ideas, revolutions, political systems, technological innovation, cultural and economic success, corporate survival, good recipes, the rise of cities, cultures, legal systems, equatorial forests, bacterial resistance, and even our own existence as a species
- **Antifragility determines the boundary between what is living and organic (or complex) and what is inert** — like a stapler on your desk vs. the human body
- The antifragile loves randomness and uncertainty — crucially, a love of errors, a certain class of errors
- Antifragility allows us to deal with the unknown, to do things without understanding them — and do them well

> "Let me be more aggressive: we are largely better at doing than we are at thinking, thanks to antifragility. I'd rather be dumb and antifragile than extremely smart and fragile, any time."

- Things around us that like a measure of stressors and volatility: economic systems, your body, your nutrition (diabetes and many modern ailments seem associated with a lack of randomness in feeding and the absence of the stressor of occasional starvation), your psyche
- There are even **financial contracts that are antifragile** — explicitly designed to benefit from market volatility
- **Antifragility and fragility are degrees on a spectrum** — just as we cannot improve health without reducing disease, or increase wealth without first decreasing losses

### Nonprediction

- By grasping the mechanisms of antifragility we can build a systematic and broad guide to **nonpredictive decision making** under uncertainty in business, politics, medicine, and life in general
- **Fragility can be measured; risk is not measurable** (outside of casinos or the minds of people who call themselves "risk experts")
- This provides a solution to the **Black Swan problem** — the impossibility of calculating the risks of consequential rare events and predicting their occurrence
- **Sensitivity to harm from volatility is tractable**, more so than forecasting the event that would cause the harm
- The proposal: stand current approaches to prediction, prognostication, and risk management on their heads
- **Simple test of asymmetry**: anything that has more upside than downside from random events (or certain shocks) is antifragile; the reverse is fragile
- In every domain, we can propose rules for moving from the fragile toward the antifragile, through reduction of fragility or harnessing of antifragility

### Deprivation of Antifragility

- **Critical insight**: antifragility is the property of all natural (and complex) systems that have survived — depriving these systems of volatility, randomness, and stressors will harm them; they will weaken, die, or blow up
- We have been **fragilizing the economy, our health, political life, education, almost everything** by suppressing randomness and volatility
- Just as spending a month in bed leads to muscle atrophy, complex systems are weakened, even killed, when deprived of stressors
- Much of our modern, structured world has been harming us with **top-down policies and contraptions** (dubbed **"Soviet-Harvard delusions"** in the book) — an insult to the antifragility of systems
- **The tragedy of modernity**: as with neurotically overprotective parents, those trying to help are often hurting us the most
- Everything **top-down fragilizes** and blocks antifragility and growth; everything **bottom-up thrives** under the right amount of stress and disorder
- The process of discovery (or innovation, or technological progress) depends on **antifragile tinkering, aggressive risk bearing** rather than formal education

### Upside at the Expense of Others

- The largest fragilizer of society and greatest generator of crises: **absence of "skin in the game"**
- Some become antifragile at the expense of others by getting the upside (or gains) from volatility, variations, and disorder and exposing others to the downside risks of losses or harm
- Such **antifragility-at-the-cost-of-fragility-of-others** is hidden — given the blindness to antifragility by the Soviet-Harvard intellectual circles, this asymmetry is rarely identified and never taught
- During the **financial crisis starting in 2008**, blowup risks-to-others were easily concealed owing to the growing complexity of modern institutions and political affairs
- In the past, people of rank or status were those who took risks and had the downside for their actions — heroes were those who did so for the sake of others; today the exact reverse is taking place
- The rise of a new class of **inverse heroes**: bureaucrats, bankers, Davos-attending members of the I.A.N.D. (International Association of Name Droppers), and academics with too much power and no real downside and/or accountability — they game the system while citizens pay the price
- At no point in history have so many **non-risk-takers** (those with no personal exposure) exerted so much control

> "The chief ethical rule is the following: Thou shalt not have antifragility at the expense of the fragility of others."

### III. The Antidote to the Black Swan

- **Black Swans** (capitalized) are large-scale unpredictable and irregular events of massive consequence — unpredicted by a certain observer, and such an unpredictor is generally called the "turkey" when he is both surprised and harmed by these events
- Taleb's claim: most of history comes from Black Swan events, while we worry about fine-tuning our understanding of the ordinary, developing models, theories, or representations that cannot possibly track or measure the possibility of these shocks
- **Black Swans hijack our brains** — making us feel we "sort of" or "almost" predicted them because they are retrospectively explainable; we don't realize the role of these Swans in life because of the **illusion of predictability**
- Life is more labyrinthine than shown in our memory — our minds turn history into something smooth and linear, which makes us underestimate randomness
- When we see randomness, we fear it and overreact; because of this fear and thirst for order, some human systems disrupt the invisible logic of things and tend to be exposed to harm from Black Swans and almost never get any benefit

> "You get pseudo-order when you seek order; you only get a measure of order and control when you embrace randomness."

- **Complex systems** are full of interdependencies — hard to detect — and nonlinear responses
- **"Nonlinear"** means that when you double the dose of, say, a medication, or double the number of employees in a factory, you don't get twice the initial effect, but rather a lot more or a lot less
- Two weekends in Philadelphia are not twice as pleasant as a single one — when plotted on a graph, the response shows as a **curve, not a straight line**
- In such an environment, simple causal associations are misplaced; it is hard to see how things work by looking at single parts
- Man-made complex systems tend to develop **cascades and runaway chains of reactions** that decrease, even eliminate, predictability and cause outsized events
- The modern world may be increasing in technological knowledge but, paradoxically, it is making things a lot more unpredictable

---

## Book I: The Antifragile: An Introduction

- The first two chapters introduce and illustrate antifragility
- **Chapter 3** introduces a distinction between the organic and the mechanical — between your cat and a washing machine
- **Chapter 4** is about how the antifragility of some comes from the fragility of others, how errors benefit some, not others — the sort of things people tend to call evolution

---

## Chapter 1: Between Damocles and Hydra

*Please cut my head off — How by some magic, colors become colors — How to lift weight in Dubai*

### Half of Life Has No Name

- **The post office package thought experiment**: You are sending a package of champagne glasses to a cousin in Central Siberia. You would stamp "fragile," "breakable," or "handle with care" on it. Now what is the exact opposite of "fragile"?
- Almost everyone answers that the opposite of "fragile" is "robust," "resilient," "solid," or something of the sort — but **this is wrong**
- The resilient, robust (and company) are items that **neither break nor improve** — you would not need to write anything on them. Have you ever seen a package with "robust" in thick green letters stamped on it?
- Logically, the exact opposite of a "fragile" parcel would be a package on which one has written **"please mishandle"** or **"please handle carelessly"** — its contents would not just be unbreakable, but would **benefit from shocks** and a wide array of trauma
- **The fragile** is the package that would be *at best* unharmed; **the robust** would be *at best* and *at worst* unharmed; and **the opposite of fragile** is therefore what is *at worst* unharmed
- The neologism **"antifragile"** was necessary as there is no simple, noncompound word in the *Oxford English Dictionary* that expresses the point of reverse fragility
- **Antifragility is not part of our consciousness** — but luckily, it is part of our ancestral behavior, our biological apparatus, and a ubiquitous property of every system that has survived
- **The blind spot is universal**: there is no word for "antifragility" in the main known languages — modern, ancient, colloquial, or slang. Even Russian (Soviet version) and Standard Brooklyn English don't seem to have a designation for it, conflating it with robustness
- Taleb checked most Indo-European languages (Latin, Greek, Romance, Slavic, Germanic, Indo-Iranian) and non-Indo-European families (Semitic, Turkic) — absent from all

> "Half of life — the interesting half of life — we don't have a name for."

- **Analogy to positive/negative**: since the opposite of *positive* is *negative*, not *neutral*, the opposite of positive fragility should be **negative fragility** (hence "antifragility"), not neutral, which would just convey robustness, strength, and unbreakability
- **Mathematically**, antifragility is fragility with a negative sign in front of it

### Please Behead Me

- If we have no common name for antifragility, we can find it through **mythological equivalence** — the expression of historical intelligence through potent metaphors
- **Damocles**: In a Roman recycled version of a Greek myth, the Sicilian tyrant Dionysius II has the fawning courtier Damocles enjoy luxury at a banquet, but with a sword hanging over his head, tied to the ceiling by a single hair from a horse's tail. The thing eventually breaks — blood, high-pitched screams, and the equivalent of ancient ambulances. **Damocles is fragile** — it is only a matter of time before the sword strikes him down
- **Phoenix**: In an ancient Semitic and Egyptian legend recycled by the Greeks, the bird with splendid colors is destroyed and reborn from its own ashes, always returning to its initial state. Phoenix is the ancient symbol of **Beirut**, the city where Taleb grew up. According to legend, Berytus has been destroyed seven times in its five-thousand-year history and come back seven times. Taleb saw the eighth episode — central Beirut was completely destroyed during the brutal civil war during his late childhood, and he also saw its eighth rebuilding
- **Beirut rebuilt even better** than its previous incarnation — the earthquake of A.D. 551 had buried the Roman law school, which was discovered as a bonus from history during reconstruction. **That's not Phoenix, but something else beyond the robust** — which brings us to the third mythological metaphor
- **Hydra**: In Greek mythology, a serpent-like creature dwelling in the lake of Lerna, near Argos, with numerous heads. Each time one is cut off, **two grow back**. So harm is what it likes. **Hydra represents antifragility**
- **The sword of Damocles** represents the side effect of power and success: you cannot rise and rule without facing continuous danger — someone will actively be working to topple you. The danger will be silent, inexorable, and discontinuous — falling abruptly after long periods of quiet
- **Black Swans will be out to get you** as you now have much more to lose — a cost of success (and growth), perhaps an unavoidable penalty of excessive success
- **The Triad's key insight**: in many situations we can measure the **strength of the string** — not the wealth and power of the dining party. This is an identifiable, measurable, and tractable vulnerability
- **Toxic growth-followed-by-a-fall**: consider how the fall of the dining guest, in response to the fall of the sword of Damocles, will bring what we now call **collateral damage**, harming others — e.g., the collapse of a large institution will have effects on society
- **Sophistication brings fragility to Black Swans**: as societies gain complexity, with more "cutting edge" sophistication and specialization, they become increasingly vulnerable to collapse. This idea has been brilliantly adumbrated by the archeologist **Joseph Tainter**
- But it does not have to be so — to counter success, you need a high offsetting dose of robustness, even high doses of antifragility. **You want to be Phoenix, or possibly Hydra. Otherwise the sword of Damocles will get you**

### On the Necessity of Naming

- **We know more than we think we do** — a lot more than we can articulate. If our formal systems of thought don't have a name for antifragility, and fight the concept whenever we use our brains, it does not mean our actions neglect it
- Our perceptions and intuitions, as expressed in deeds, can be **superior to what we know and tabulate**, discuss in words, and teach in a classroom
- The potent notion of the **apophatic** (what cannot be explicitly said, or directly described, in our current vocabulary) will be discussed later
- **Guy Deutscher's** *Through the Language Glass*: many primitive populations, without being color-blind, have verbal designations for only two or three colors — yet when given a simple test, they can successfully match strings to their corresponding colors. They detect differences between nuances of the rainbow but do not express them in their vocabularies. These populations are **culturally, though not biologically, color-blind**
- Just as we are intellectually, not organically, **antifragility-blind** — you need the name "blue" for the construction of a narrative, but not when you engage in action
- **Many colors we take for granted had no name for a long time** and had no names in the central texts in Western culture. Ancient Mediterranean texts (Greek and Semitic) had a reduced vocabulary of colors polarized around the dark and the light — Homer and his contemporaries were limited to about three or four main colors: black, white, and some indeterminate part of the rainbow, often subsumed as red or yellow
- **Guy Deutscher** pointed out that the ancients even lacked words for something as elementary as blue — explaining Homer's recurring reference to the **"wine-dark sea"** (*oinopa ponton*), which has been quite puzzling to readers
- **William Gladstone**, British Prime Minister, first made the discovery of limited ancient color vocabulary in the 1850s, in an impressive seventeen-hundred-page treatise on Homer. Gladstone attributed this limitation to a cross-generational training of the eye, but regardless, people were shown to be able to identify the nuances — unless physically color-blind
- Gladstone showed remarkable prescience: he figured out that the *Iliad* corresponds to a true story (the city of Troy had not been discovered yet), and of great relevance to this book, he was insistent upon a **balanced fiscal budget** — fiscal deficits have proven to be a prime source of fragility in social and economic systems

### Proto-Antifragility

- There have been names for two **starter-antifragility concepts** — two precursor applications that cover some special cases, mild aspects of antifragility limited to the medical field
- **Mithridatism**: According to legend, **Mithridates IV**, king of Pontus in Asia Minor, while hiding after his father's assassination, got himself some protection against poisoning by ingesting sub-lethal doses of toxic material in progressively larger quantities. He later incorporated the process into a complicated religious ritual. But this immunity got him in trouble later — when his attempt to take his own life by poisoning failed ("having fortified himself against the drugs of others"), he had to ask for a sword blow instead
- The method named **Antidotum Mithridatium**, celebrated by Celsus, the ancient world's famous doctor, became rather fashionable in Rome — until it brought complications for the emperor Nero's attempts at matricide
- **Nero and Agrippina**: Nero wanted to kill his mother Agrippina, who was Caligula's sister and the alleged lover of the philosopher Seneca. Agrippina, suspecting Nero had a contract on her, **Mithridatized herself** against the poisons available to her son's underlings. Like Mithridates, she eventually died by more mechanical methods (assassins)
- **Lesson**: one cannot be robust against everything — and two thousand years later, nobody has found a method for getting "fortified" against swords
- **Mithridatization** = the result of an exposure to a small dose of a substance that, over time, makes one immune to additional, larger quantities. It is the approach used in **vaccination and allergy medicine**. It is not quite antifragility — still at the more modest level of robustness — but a hint that perhaps being deprived of poison makes us fragile and that the road to robustification starts with a modicum of harm
- **Hormesis**: When the poisonous substance, in some dose, makes you **better off overall** — one step up from robustness. A word coined by pharmacologists: when a small dose of a harmful substance is actually beneficial for the organism, acting as medicine
- A little bit of an otherwise offending substance, not too much, acts to benefit the organism and make it better overall as it triggers some **overreaction**
- This was not interpreted at the time in the sense of "gains from harm" so much as **"harm is dose dependent"** or **"medicine is dose dependent"** — the interest to scientists has been in the nonlinearity of the dose-response
- **Hormesis was well known by the ancients** (and like the color blue was known but not expressed). It was only in **1888** that it was first "scientifically" described (though still not given a name) by a German toxicologist, **Hugo Schulz**, who observed that small doses of poison stimulate the growth of yeast while larger doses cause harm
- Some researchers hold that the benefits of vegetables may not be so much in what we call "vitamins" or some other rationalizing theories, but in the following: **plants protect themselves from harm and fend off predators with poisonous substances** that, ingested by us in the right quantities, may stimulate our organisms — limited, low-dose poisoning triggers healthy benefits
- **Caloric restriction** (permanent or episodic) activates healthy reactions and switches that, among other benefits, lengthen life expectancy in laboratory animals. It looks like such restriction makes humans healthier (and may also improve their sense of humor). But since abundance would bring the opposite effect, this can be interpreted as: **too much regular food is bad for you**, and depriving humans of the stressor of hunger may make them live less than their full potential
- **All hormesis seems to be doing is reestablishing the natural dosage for food and hunger in humans** — hormesis is the norm, and its absence is what hurts us
- Hormesis lost scientific respect after the 1930s because some people mistakenly associated it with **homeopathy** — but the association was unfair, as the mechanisms are extremely different. Homeopathy has shown little empirical backing; hormesis, as a phenomenon, has ample scientific evidence to back it up
- **Larger point**: depriving systems of stressors, vital stressors, is not necessarily a good thing, and can be **downright harmful**

### Domain Independence Is Domain Dependent

- The idea that systems may need some stress and agitation has been **missed by those who grasp it in one area and not in another** — this is **domain dependence** of our minds, a "domain" being an area or category of activity
- Some people can understand an idea in one domain (say, medicine) and **fail to recognize it in another** (say, socioeconomic life). Or they get it in the classroom, but not in the more complicated texture of the street. Humans somehow fail to recognize situations outside the contexts in which they usually learn about them
- **The Dubai banker anecdote**: Taleb saw a fellow who looked like a banker have a uniformed porter carry his luggage in a hotel driveway, then fifteen minutes later saw the same banker lifting free weights at the gym, trying to replicate natural exercises using kettlebells **as if he were swinging a suitcase**. Domain dependence is pervasive
- **Medical domain dependence**: Mithridatization and hormesis can be known in (some) medical circles and missed in other applications such as socioeconomic life. Even within medicine, some get it here and miss it there — the same doctor might recommend exercise so you "get tougher," and a few minutes later write a prescription for antibiotics in response to a trivial infection so you "don't get sick"
- **Federal Reserve domain dependence**: Ask a U.S. citizen if some semi-governmental agency with a great deal of independence should control the price of cars, morning newspapers, and Malbec wine — he would jump in anger. But then ask if that same government agency should control foreign exchange or interest rates — same reaction: this is not France. Yet the **Federal Reserve Bank** is in the business of controlling and managing the price of another good, called the lending rate (the interest rate in the economy). The libertarian presidential candidate **Ron Paul** was called a crank for suggesting the abolition of the Federal Reserve
- **Translation blindness**: Imagine someone gifted in learning languages but unable to transfer concepts from one tongue to another — he would need to relearn "chair" or "love" or "apple pie" every time he acquires a new language. We are all similarly handicapped, unable to recognize the same idea when it is presented in a different context
- We are doomed to be deceived by the most superficial part of things, the packaging, the gift wrapping — this is why we don't see antifragility in places that are obvious, too obvious
- It is not part of the accepted way of thinking about success, economic growth, or innovation that these may result only from **overcompensation against stressors** — nor do we see this overcompensation at work elsewhere
- **Domain dependence** is also why it has been difficult for many researchers to realize that uncertainty, incomplete understanding, disorder, and volatility are **members of the same close family**
- This lack of translation is a mental handicap that comes with being human — we will only start to attain wisdom or rationality when we make an effort to overcome and break through it

---

## Chapter 2: Overcompensation and Overreaction Everywhere

*Is it easy to write on a Heathrow runway? — Try to get the Pope to ban your work — How to beat up an economist (but not too hard, just enough to go to jail)*

### Post-Traumatic Growth

- Taleb's own domain dependence was revealed while sitting in the office of **David Halpern**, a U.K. government advisor and policy maker, who informed him of a phenomenon called **post-traumatic growth** — the opposite of post-traumatic stress syndrome, by which people harmed by past events surpass themselves
- Taleb had never heard about it before, and to his great shame, had never made the effort to think of its existence — there is a small literature but it is not advertised outside a narrow discipline
- We hear about the more lurid **post-traumatic stress disorder**, not post-traumatic growth, in the intellectual and so-called learned vocabulary — but popular culture has an awareness of its equivalent, revealed in the expression **"it builds character."** So do the ancient Mediterranean classics, along with grandmothers
- **Intellectuals tend to focus on negative responses from randomness** (fragility) rather than the positive ones (antifragility) — this is not just in psychology: it prevails across the board

### Innovation from Necessity

- **How do you innovate? First, try to get in trouble** — serious, but not terminal, trouble
- Taleb holds — as a conviction, not speculation — that **innovation and sophistication spark from initial situations of necessity**, in ways that go far beyond the satisfaction of such necessity (from the unintended side effects of, say, an initial invention or attempt at invention)
- Classical thoughts on this subject: a Latin saying that sophistication is born out of hunger (**artificia docuit fames**). In Ovid, difficulty is what wakes up the genius (**ingenium mala saepe movent**), which translates in Brooklyn English into "When life gives you a lemon..."
- **The excess energy released from overreaction to setbacks is what innovates!**
- This message from the ancients contradicts modern methods and ideas of innovation and progress on many levels — we tend to think innovation comes from bureaucratic funding, through planning, or by putting people through a Harvard Business School class by one Highly Decorated Professor of Innovation and Entrepreneurship (who never innovated anything) or hiring a consultant (who never innovated anything)
- This is a fallacy — note the disproportionate contribution of **uneducated technicians and entrepreneurs** to various technological leaps, from the Industrial Revolution to the emergence of Silicon Valley
- Despite the visibility of the counterevidence, and the wisdom you can pick up free of charge from the ancients (or grandmothers), moderns try today to create inventions from situations of **comfort, safety, and predictability** instead of accepting the notion that "necessity really is the mother of invention"
- **Cato the Censor**, the great Roman statesman, looked at comfort, almost any form of comfort, as a road to waste. He did not like it when we had it too easy, as he worried about the weakening of the will — and the softening he feared was not just at the personal level: an entire society can fall ill
- **Abundance is harder for us to handle than scarcity** — consider that as Taleb writes, we are living in a debt crisis: the world as a whole has never been richer, and it has never been more heavily in debt, living off borrowed money. The record shows that for society, the richer we become, the harder it gets to live within our means

### Automation and the Loss of Challenge

- **Cato** would have smiled hearing about the recently observed effect in aeronautics that the automation of airplanes is **underchallenging pilots**, making flying too comfortable for them — dangerously comfortable
- The dulling of the pilot's attention and skills from too *little* challenge is indeed causing deaths from flying accidents
- Part of the problem is an **FAA regulation** that forced the industry to increase its reliance on automated flying — but thankfully, the same FAA finally figured out the problem; it has recently found that pilots often **"abdicate too much responsibility to automated systems"**

### How to Win a Horse Race

- **The best horses lose when they compete with slower ones, and win against better rivals** — undercompensation from the absence of a stressor, inverse hormesis, absence of challenge, degrades the best of the best
- In Baudelaire's poem, "The albatross's giant wings prevent him from walking" — many do better in Calculus 103 than Calculus 101
- **Overcompensation hides in the most unlikely places**: if tired after an intercontinental flight, go to the gym for some exertion instead of resting. If you need something urgently done, give the task to the busiest (or second busiest) person in the office. Most humans manage to squander their free time — the busier they get, the more active they are at other tasks. Overcompensation, here again
- **Whispering, not shouting**: Taleb discovered a trick when giving lectures — rather than speaking with the fake articulation of TV announcers or dancing on stage, he resolved to **whisper, not shout**. Better to be slightly inaudible, less clear
- When he was a pit trader (standing in a crowded arena shouting and screaming in a continuous auction), he learned that the noise produced by the person is **inverse to the pecking order** — as with mafia dons, the most powerful traders were the least audible
- One should have enough self-control to make the audience work hard to listen, which causes them to switch into **intellectual overdrive**
- There is empirical evidence of the effect of **"disfluency"**: mental effort moves us into higher gear, activating more vigorous and more analytical brain machinery
- **Peter Drucker** (management guru) and **Jacques Lacan** (psychoanalyst), two persons who mesmerized crowds the most in their respective areas, were the antithesis of the polished-swanky speaker or the consonant-trained television announcer
- The same mechanism of overcompensation makes us **concentrate better in the presence of a modicum of background random noise**

## Chapter 2: Overcompensation and Overreaction Everywhere (continued)

### Antifragile Responses as Redundancy

- When Taleb heard "post-traumatic growth" during his London visit, it hit him: **antifragile hormetic responses were just a form of redundancy**, and all the ideas of Mother Nature converged — Nature likes to overinsure itself
- **Layers of redundancy** are the central risk management property of natural systems — we humans have two kidneys, extra spare parts, and extra capacity in many organs (lungs, neural system, arterial apparatus)
- Human design tends to be spare and inversely redundant — we engage in **debt**, which is the opposite of redundancy (fifty thousand in extra cash under the mattress is redundancy; owing the bank an equivalent amount is the opposite)
- **Redundancy is ambiguous** because it seems like a waste if nothing unusual happens — except that something unusual *usually* happens
- Redundancy is not necessarily defensive; it can be **extremely aggressive** — if you have extra inventory of fertilizers and there's a shortage due to disruptions in China, you can sell at a huge premium
- **Overcompensation is just a form of redundancy** — an additional head for the Hydra is no different from an extra kidney for humans, or extra cash in the bank
- A system that overcompensates is necessarily in **overshooting mode**, building extra capacity and strength in anticipation of a worse outcome
- **Redundancy is not defensive; it is more like investment than insurance** — tell the next MBA analyst that what they call "inefficient" is often very efficient

### The Lucretius Problem

- Risk management professionals look at **worst-case scenarios** from past data ("stress testing"), but they never notice that this so-called worst case *exceeded the worst case at the time*
- Taleb calls this mental defect **the Lucretius problem**, after the Latin poetic philosopher who wrote that the fool believes the tallest mountain in the world will be equal to the tallest one he has observed
- In **Pharaonic Egypt**, bureaucratic scribes tracked the high-water mark of the Nile and used it as a future worst-case estimate
- The **Fukushima nuclear reactor** was built to withstand the worst past historical earthquake — the builders never imagined much worse
- **Alan Greenspan** offered Congress the classic "it never happened before"
- Nature, unlike Fragilista Greenspan, prepares for what has not happened before, **assuming worse harm is possible**
- If humans fight the last war, **nature fights the next one** — your body is more imaginative about the future than you are
- **Maximum lifts in weightlifting** illustrate the point: Taleb trained with "Lenny Cake," focusing solely on improving one's past maximum in a single lift — the body *predicts* it will need to lift more next time, building additional strength automatically

### On the Antifragility of Riots, Love, and Other Unexpected Beneficiaries of Stress

- Once one overcomes domain dependence, the phenomenon of overcompensation appears **ubiquitous**
- **Seneca** in *De clemencia* on the inverse effect of punishments: "Repeated punishment, while it crushes the hatred of a few, stirs the hatred of all ... just as trees that have been trimmed throw out again countless branches"
- **Revolutions feed on repression**, growing heads faster and faster as one literally cuts a few off

> "The higher you build your barricades, the stronger we become." — Irish revolutionary song

- Political movements and rebellions can be **highly antifragile** — the sucker game is to try to repress them using brute force
- One of the most antifragile things outside economic life is **obsessive love (or hate)** — a refractory passion that overreacts and overcompensates for impediments
- In Proust's *La recherche*, Swann falls for Odette — her elusive behavior fuels his obsession. In Buzzati's *Un amore*, a middle-aged Milanese man falls for a dancer who exploits him; the more she mistreats him, the more he exposes himself
- Like tormenting love, **some thoughts are so antifragile** that you feed them by trying to get rid of them — the more energy you put into trying to control your ideas, the more your ideas end up controlling you

### Please Ban My Book: The Antifragility of Information

- **Information is antifragile** — it feeds more on attempts to harm it than on efforts to promote it
- The wily Venetians knew how to spread information by disguising it as a secret — the more you insist it remain a secret, the more it will spread
- Books and ideas are **antifragile to attacks** — as Marcus Aurelius wrote, "fire feeds on obstacles." Banned books thrive: Graham Greene's *The Power and the Glory*, Henry Miller, *Madame Bovary*, *Lady Chatterley's Lover*
- The **Ayn Rand phenomenon**: *Atlas Shrugged* and *The Fountainhead* have been read for more than half a century by millions, *in spite of* — or most likely *thanks to* — brutally nasty reviews
- **Criticism is an unfaked badge of attention** signaling a book is not boring — and boring is the only truly bad thing for a book
- **Heuristic**: to estimate the quality of research, take the caliber of the highest detractor, or the caliber of the lowest detractor whom the author answers in print — whichever is lower
- Taleb's great-grandfather **Nicolas Ghosn**, a wily politician who stayed permanently in power, told his grandson from his deathbed: "My son, I am very disappointed in you. I never hear anything wrong said about you. You have proven yourself incapable of generating envy."

### Get Another Job

- It is not possible to stamp out criticism; if it harms you, **get out** — it is easier to change jobs than control your reputation
- Some jobs and professions are **fragile to reputational harm** — in the age of the Internet, these jobs aren't worth having. Focus on altering your exposure
- A writer is antifragile; most modernistic professions are usually not
- Someone earning close to minimum wage — a construction worker or taxi driver — does not overly depend on his reputation and is **free to have his own opinions**. A midlevel bank employee with a mortgage would be **completely a prisoner** of the value system
- **Heuristic for detecting independence**: those who dress outrageously are robust or even antifragile in reputation; those in suits and ties are fragile to information about them
- Large corporations and governments that try to control information and "reinstill confidence" are **revealing their fragility** — information is merciless
- When you don't have debt, you don't care about your reputation in economics circles — **people lend the most to those who need them the least**

---

## Chapter 3: The Cat and the Washing Machine

*Stress is knowledge (and knowledge is stress) — The organic and the mechanical — No translator needed, for now — Waking up the animal in us, after two hundred years of modernity*

### The Organic vs. the Mechanical

- **Bold conjecture**: everything that has life in it is to some extent antifragile (but not the reverse) — it looks like the secret of life is antifragility
- The natural — the biological — is both antifragile and fragile, depending on the source and range of variation. A human body can benefit from stressors (to get stronger), but only to a point
- Your bones get denser when episodic stress is applied to them — **Wolff's Law** (1892)
- A dish, a car, an inanimate object will not — these may be robust but cannot be intrinsically antifragile
- **Brent Carey's 2011 experiment**: carbon nanotubes arranged in a certain manner produce a self-strengthening response previously unseen in synthetic materials — crossing the boundary between living and inanimate
- We can use the distinction as a marker between living and nonliving: **the artificial needs to be antifragile for us to be able to use it as tissue**
- The **phenomenon of aging is misunderstood** — failures to self-repair come largely from **maladjustment**, either too few stressors or too little time for recovery. Much of aging comes from a misunderstanding of the effect of comfort — a disease of civilization
- In a natural environment, people die without aging — or after a very short period of aging. **Artificial aging comes from stifling internal antifragility**

### The Complex

- The effective distinction is between **noncomplex and complex systems** — more effective than biological/nonbiological
- Many things such as society, economic activities, markets, and cultural behavior are man-made but grow on their own to reach self-organization — closer to the cat than to the washing machine, but **mistaken for washing machines**
- Artificial, man-made mechanical contraptions are *complicated*, but not "complex" — they don't have interdependencies. Push a button, get an exact response
- With **complex systems, interdependencies are severe** — remove a specific animal from a food chain and you get cascading complications
- In the complex world, the notion of "cause" itself is suspect — it is either nearly impossible to detect or not really defined

### Stressors Are Information

- The crux of complex systems is that they **convey information to their component parts through stressors** — your body gets information about the environment not through your intelligence, but through stress, via hormones or other messengers
- Your bones get stronger when subjected to gravity; they become weaker in a space station with zero gravity. Your skin callouses from bicycle riding, tans in summer
- **Errors and their consequences are information**; for small children, pain is the only risk management information
- For complex systems, everything is about information — this is **causal opacity**: it is hard to see the arrow from cause to consequence

### Equilibrium, Not Again

- For the complexity theorist **Stuart Kaufman**, the social scientist's goal of "equilibrium" can be death
- *For the nonorganic, noncomplex, equilibrium happens in a state of inertia. So for something organic, equilibrium only happens with death*
- **Dissipative structures** (Ilya Prigogine): organisms and dynamic systems exist permanently "far from equilibrium" — a state of normalcy requires volatility, randomness, and stress

### Crimes Against Children

- We are **committing crimes against life** for the sake of eliminating volatility and variation
- One in ten Americans beyond high school is on some kind of antidepressant — but mood swings are a second source of intelligence, perhaps even the first
- Had Prozac been available last century, Baudelaire's "spleen," Poe's moods, Sylvia Plath's poetry would have been silenced

### Punished by Translation

- You pick up a language best through situational difficulty, from error to error — success, wealth, and technology make this mode of acquisition much harder
- The best way to learn a language may be an episode of jail in a foreign country — Taleb's friend Chad Garcia improved his Russian in a Moscow hospital quarantine

### Touristification

- **Touristification**: the systematic removal of uncertainty and randomness from things, making matters highly predictable in their smallest details — all for comfort, convenience, and efficiency
- What a tourist is to an adventurer, touristification is to life — it converts activities into the equivalent of a script
- The guilty parties: the education system, planning, teleological scientific research, gym machines, the electronic calendar
- The worst touristification: **the life we moderns lead in captivity during our leisure hours** — Friday night opera, scheduled parties. A golden jail

### The Secret Thirst for Chance

- If you are alive — something deep in your soul likes a certain measure of randomness and disorder
- **If I could predict what my day would exactly look like, I would feel a little bit dead**
- In an ancestral habitat we were prompted by natural stimuli — fear, hunger, desire — that made us fit for our environment
- An environment with variability does not expose us to **chronic stress injury** — walking on uneven terrain means no two steps are identical, unlike the gym machine forcing endless identical repetitions
- **Much of modern life is preventable chronic stress injury**

---

## Chapter 4: What Kills Me Makes Others Stronger

*Antifragility for one is fragility for someone else — Where we introduce the idea that we think too much, do very little — Fail for others to succeed — One day you may get a thank-you note*

### Antifragility by Layers

- **The antifragility of some comes necessarily at the expense of the fragility of others** — the sacrifices of some fragile units are often necessary for the well-being of other units or the whole
- **The fragility of every startup is necessary for the economy to be antifragile** — that's what makes entrepreneurship work
- **Restaurants** are individually fragile (they compete and fail), but the collective of local restaurants is **antifragile for that very reason** — had they been individually robust, the overall business would deliver nothing better than Soviet cafeteria food
- Some parts *on the inside* of a system may be required to be fragile to make the system antifragile as a result

### Evolution and Unpredictability

- Hormesis is a metaphor; **antifragility is a phenomenon** — Mithridatization and hormesis were "proto-antifragility" concepts, even a bit naive
- There is a different, stronger variety of antifragility linked to **evolution** — operating at the informational level (genes are information). Unlike with hormesis, the unit does not get stronger; **it dies**. But it accomplishes a transfer of benefits
- **Robert Trivers** figured out the competition between gene and organism in his idea of the "selfish gene"
- Evolution only works **because of its antifragility** — it is in love with stressors, randomness, uncertainty, and disorder
- **Organisms need to die for nature to be antifragile** — nature is opportunistic, ruthless, and selfish
- An **immortal organism** would need to predict the future perfectly. But by letting organisms go one lifespan at a time with modifications between generations, nature does not need to predict — **every random event brings its own antidote in the form of ecological variation**
- **Evolution benefits from randomness by two routes**: randomness in mutations, and randomness in the environment

### Organisms Are Populations and Populations Are Organisms

- Physicist turned geneticist **Antoine Danchin**: analysis needs to accommodate layering and hierarchies
- **Fractal self-similarity** (Benoit Mandelbrot): a tree has branches that look like small trees, which have branches like even smaller trees — similarly, cells have populations of molecules, organisms have populations of cells, species have populations of organisms
- A strengthening mechanism for the species comes at the expense of some organisms; the organism strengthens at the expense of some cells
- When you drink a poisonous substance in small amounts, evolutionary processes *within* your system replace bad proteins with stronger, younger ones — a process called **autophagy** — a purely evolutionary process that selects and kills the weakest for fitness

### Thank You, Errors

- **When you are fragile**, you depend on things following the exact planned course — deviations are more harmful than helpful
- **When you are antifragile**, deviations are more helpful than harmful — you don't care about the dispersion of outcomes
- The random element in trial and error is not quite random — **error is used as a source of information**. Every trial tells you what *does not* work, zooming in on a solution

### Learning from the Mistakes of Others

- It is often the mistakes of others that benefit the rest of us
- **Henry Petroski**: had the *Titanic* not had that famous accident, we would have kept building larger ocean liners and the next disaster would have been even more tragic
- **Every plane crash makes the next flight safer** — aviation is set up to exploit small errors, independent from each other
- But **the economic system is not antifragile** as presently built — globalized economic systems operate as one, errors spread and compound
- **If every plane crash makes the next one less likely, every bank crash makes the next one more likely** — we need to eliminate the contagion-producing type of error

### How to Become Mother Teresa

- Variability causes mistakes and adaptations; it also allows you to know who your friends are — sometimes you only know about someone's character after you harm them with an error for which you are solely responsible
- **A loser** is someone who, after making a mistake, doesn't introspect, doesn't exploit it, feels embarrassed and defensive rather than enriched with new information, and tries to explain why he made the mistake rather than moving on

### Why the Aggregate Hates the Individual

- The economy has equivalent layering: individuals, artisans, small firms, departments within corporations, industries, the regional economy, and the general economy
- For the economy to be antifragile and undergo evolution, **every single individual business must necessarily be fragile** — exposed to breaking
- **Every time you use a coffeemaker**, you are benefiting from the fragility of the coffeemaking entrepreneur who failed — he failed in order to help put the superior merchandise on your kitchen counter
- Natural and nature-like systems want some **overconfidence** on the part of individual economic agents — the overestimation of their chances of success and underestimation of risks of failure. This local (not global) overconfidence is healthy for the economy
- With bailouts, governments favor firms that are large enough to require being saved to avoid contagion — this is **transferring fragility from the collective to the unfit**

### What Does Not Kill Me Kills Others

- Nietzsche's "what does not kill me makes me stronger" can be misinterpreted — it could mean "what did not kill me did *not* make me stronger, but spared me *because* I am stronger than others; but it killed others and the average population is now stronger because the weak are gone"
- The surviving cohort is clearly stronger than the initial one — but not quite the individuals, since the weaker ones died. **Someone paid a price for the system to improve**

### Me and Us

- The tension between individual and collective interests is new in history — in the past it was dealt with by the near irrelevance of individuals. Sacrifice for the group is behind the notion of **heroism**
- As a humanist, Taleb stands against the antifragility of systems at the expense of individuals — the Enlightenment brought the individual to the fore with rights, freedom, independence, and privacy

### National Entrepreneur Day

- Heroism and the respect it commands is a form of **compensation by society for those who take risks for others** — entrepreneurship is a risky and heroic activity, necessary for growth
- Someone who did *not* find something is providing others with knowledge, the best knowledge — that of *absence* (what does not work) — yet he gets little or no credit for it
- Modern society should be treating ruined entrepreneurs the same way we honor dead soldiers — **there is no such thing as a failed entrepreneur** any more than there is a failed soldier or failed scientific researcher
- Taleb's dream: **National Entrepreneur Day** with the message: "Most of you will fail, disrespected, impoverished, but we are grateful for the risks you are taking. *You are at the source of our antifragility.* Our nation thanks you."

---

## Book II: Modernity and the Denial of Antifragility

- **"Volatility"** comes from *volare*, "to fly" in Latin — depriving political and other systems of volatility harms them, causing eventually greater volatility of the cascading type
- Book II deals with the **fragility that comes from the denial of hormesis** — how we hurt systems by playing conductor, fragilizing social and economic systems by denying them stressors and randomness
- **Procrustes** was an inn-keeper in Greek mythology who cut the limbs of those too tall and stretched those too short to make travelers fit in his bed — treating an organism like a simple machine is exactly like a Procrustean bed

---

## Chapter 5: The Souk and the Office Building

*The Reds and the Whites all go to Zurich — War is not a prison — The turkey's thwarted projects — Remember we are in Extremistan*

### Two Types of Professions

- **John and George**: identical twin brothers from Cyprus living in Greater London. John is a bank clerk (personnel department, 25 years). George drives a black taxi
- John has a perfectly **predictable income** — monthly paycheck, annual vacation, gold watch every 25 years. Until the banking crisis, when he realized his job could be "made redundant." At fifty, he would never recover
- George the taxi driver has **extremely variable income** — some days several hundred pounds, some days nothing. But year after year, he averages the same as John. He has never had a single day without a fare in 25 years. He is his own boss, and can continue into his eighties
- **The central illusion in life: that randomness is risky, that it is a bad thing — and that eliminating randomness is done by eliminating randomness**
- Artisanal careers harbor a bit of antifragility: small variations make them adapt and change continuously, open to gifts and positive surprises — **free options, the hallmark of antifragility**
- For a self-employed person, a small mistake is information that directs his adaptive approach; for an employee like John, a mistake goes into his permanent record
- **The centralized state resembles John's income; the city-state model resembles George's** — John has one large employer, George many small ones. One has the illusion of stability but is fragile; the other has the illusion of variability but is robust and even antifragile
- **The more variability you observe in a system, the less Black Swan-prone it is**

### Lenin in Zurich

- **Switzerland is the most antifragile place on the planet** — it benefits from shocks that take place in the rest of the world
- Lenin concocted his project of centralized state control while living in Zurich; Nabokov, the emigre Russian aristocrat and victim of Lenin, later spent decades in the same lakefront hotel in Montreux
- Switzerland shelters both the reds and the whites — all manner of financial and political refugees. It is economically the most robust place on the planet, and has been for centuries
- The most stable country in the world **does not have a government** — or rather, it does not have a large *central* government. What governs them is entirely bottom-up: municipal entities called cantons, near-sovereign mini-states united in a confederation
- Bottom-up form of dictatorship provides **protection against the romanticism of utopias** — the process is highly unintellectual, even downright puny, but the system produces stability at every possible level

### Bottom-Up Variations

- **Bottom-up variations** — the type of political volatility within a municipality — is not scalable (not *invariant* under scale transformation). A large state does not behave like a gigantic municipality, much as a baby human does not resemble a smaller adult
- A cluster of municipalities with charming provincial enmities aggregates to a quite benign and stable state — **Switzerland shows stability at the aggregate level, as the ensemble of cantons produces a solid system**, like the cab driver's income
- **"Stalin could not have existed in a municipality"** — small is more antifragile; the large is doomed to breaking
- Biology plays a role in a municipal environment — an administrator is shielded from having to feel the sting of shame (a biological reaction) for overspending and failures. Eye contact with one's peers changes one's behavior
- Lobbyists cannot exist in a municipality or small region — centralization of power (like the European Commission in Brussels) creates a much larger payoff from lobbying at low cost
- The Italian legal philosopher **Bruno Leoni** argued in favor of the robustness of judge-based law (owing to its diversity) as compared to explicit and rigid codified law — true, the choice of a court could be a lottery, but it helps prevent large-scale mistakes
- Switzerland illustrates the **natural antifragility of political systems** — stability is achieved by managing noise, having a mechanism for letting it run its natural course, not by minimizing it
- Switzerland is perhaps the **most successful country in history**, yet it has traditionally had a very low level of university education compared to the rest of the rich nations — its system, even in banking, was based on **apprenticeship models**, nearly vocational rather than theoretical: on **techne** (crafts and know how), not **episteme** (book knowledge, know what)

### Away from Extremistan

- There is a certain **mathematical property** to bottom-up volatility and to the volatility of natural systems — it generates the kind of randomness Taleb calls **Mediocristan**: plenty of variations that might be scary, but tend to cancel out in the aggregate (over time, or over the collection of municipalities that constitute the larger confederation or entity)
- The alternative is the unruly one called **Extremistan**, in which you have mostly stability and occasionally large chaos — errors there have large consequences
- **Mediocristan** has a lot of small variations, not a single one of which is extreme; **Extremistan** has few variations, but those that take place are extreme
- One fluctuates, the other jumps. One has a lot of small variations, the other varies in lumps — just like the income of the taxi driver compared to that of the bank employee. **The two types of randomness are qualitatively distinct**
- **Caloric intake analogy**: your caloric intake is from Mediocristan — if you add the calories you consume in a year, not a single day will represent much of the total (say, more than 0.5 percent of the total). You cannot double your weight in a single day, not even a month — but you *can* double your net worth or lose half of it in a single moment
- **Novel sales** illustrate Extremistan: more than half of sales (and perhaps 90 percent of profits) tend to come from the top 0.1 percent — the one-in-a-thousand event is dominant
- Financial matters and other economic matters tend to be from **Extremistan**, just like history, which moves by discontinuities and jumps from one state to another
- **Figure 3** illustrates how antifragile systems are hurt when deprived of their natural variations (mostly thanks to naive intervention):
  - **First graph**: Municipal noise / distributed variations in the souks — volatile; it fluctuates but does not sink (like a taxi driver's income)
  - **Second graph**: Centralized or human-managed systems — sinks without significant fluctuations outside episodes of turmoil (like an employee's income). Shows moves taking place from cascade to cascade, or Black Swan to Black Swan
- Human overintervention to smooth or control processes causes a switch from one kind of system (**Mediocristan**) into another (**Extremistan**) — this effect applies to all manner of systems with constrained volatility: health, politics, economics, even someone's mood with and without Prozac
- In the long run the second system will be **far more volatile** — but volatility comes in lumps. When we constrain the first system we tend to get the second outcome
- In **Extremistan** predictability is very low — in the second, pseudo-smooth kind of randomness, mistakes appear to be rare, but they will be large, often devastating when they occur
- Anything locked into planning tends to fail precisely because of these attributes — it is quite a myth that **planning helps corporations**; the world is too random and unpredictable to base a policy on visibility of the future. **What survives comes from the interplay of some fitness and environmental conditions**

### The Great Turkey Problem

- In **Extremistan**, one is prone to be fooled by the properties of the past and get the story exactly backwards
- It is easy, looking at the second graph of Figure 3, before the big jump down, to believe the system is now safe — particularly when the system has made a progressive switch from the "scary" type of visibly volatile randomness at left to the apparently safe right. **It looks like a drop in volatility — and it is not**
- **The Turkey metaphor** (adapted from Bertrand Russell): A turkey is fed for a thousand days by a butcher; every day confirms to its staff of analysts that butchers love turkeys "with increased statistical confidence." The butcher will keep feeding the turkey until a few days before Thanksgiving — then comes that day when it is really not a good idea to be a turkey
- With the butcher surprising it, the turkey will have a **revision of belief** — right when its confidence in the statement that *the butcher loves turkeys* is maximal and "it is very quiet" and soothingly predictable in the life of the turkey
- The key: such a surprise will be a **Black Swan event** — but just for the turkey, not for the butcher
- The **mother of all harmful mistakes**: mistaking absence of evidence (of harm) for evidence of absence — a mistake that tends to prevail in intellectual circles and the social sciences
- Our mission in life becomes simply **"how not to be a turkey"** — or, if possible, how to be a turkey in reverse — antifragile. "Not being a turkey" starts with figuring out the difference between **true and manufactured stability**
- When constrained, volatility-choked systems explode — fitting example: the removal of the **Baath Party**, with the abrupt toppling of Saddam Hussein and his regime in 2003 by the United States. More than a hundred thousand persons died, and ten years later, the place is still a mess

### The Collective vs. the Individual (Chapter 4 continued)

- The unit in **traditional cultures** is the collective — it could be perceived to be harmed by the behavior of an individual: the honor of the family is sullied when a daughter becomes pregnant, or a family member engages in large-scale financial swindles and Ponzi schemes
- In rural France as recently as the late nineteenth/early twentieth century, it was common for someone to spend all his savings to erase the debts of a remote cousin — a practice called **passer l'éponge** (literally, to use a sponge to erase the liability from the chalkboard), to preserve the dignity and good name of the extended family. It was perceived as a duty
- The system needs to be there for the individual to survive — one needs to be careful in glorifying one interest against others in the presence of **interdependence and complexity**
- In the **Cosa Nostra** (Sicilian mafia), the designation "man of honor" (*uomo d'onore*) implies that the person caught by police would remain silent and not rat on his friends, regardless of benefits — life in prison is preferable to a plea that entails hurting other members. **The tribe comes before the individual**
- What broke the back of the mafia was the recent generation of **plea bargainers** — "honor" in the mafia is limited to in-group solidarity; they otherwise lie, and kill people from behind, which on the east side of the Mediterranean is considered the purest form of cowardice
- We humans may have to be **self-centered at the expense of other species**, at the risk of ecological fragility, if it insures our survival — our interests as a human race prevail over those of nature, and we can tolerate some inefficiency and fragility to protect individuals, although sacrificing nature too much may eventually hurt ourselves
- The trade-off between collective and individual: an economy **cannot survive without breaking individual eggs**; protection is harmful, and constraining the forces of evolution to benefit individuals does not seem required. But we can shield individuals from starvation, provide some social protection, and give them respect

### Overconfidence: Benign vs. Harmful

- Psychologists label "overconfidence" a disease, blinding people to the odds of success in ventures — but there is a difference between the **benign, heroic type of risk taking** that is beneficial to others (in the antifragile case) and the nastier modern type related to **negative Black Swans** (e.g., the overconfidence of "scientists" computing risks of harm from the Fukushima reactor)
- In the case of the former, what they call overconfidence is **a good thing, not something to medicate**
- Compare entrepreneurs to the **beancounting managers** of companies who climb the ladder of hierarchy with hardly any real downside — their cohort is rarely at risk
- What Erasmus called **ingratitudo vulgi** (the ingratitude of the masses) is increasing in the age of globalization and the Internet

> "I have noticed that the more people glorify the entrepreneur as an abstraction, the more they will scorn an actual one they meet." — Jean-Louis Rheault

### Twelve Thousand Years

- We started with the example of Switzerland; now Taleb moves farther east to the **northern Levant** — roughly today's northern part of Syria and Lebanon
- The northern Levant stayed perhaps the **most prosperous province in the history of mankind** over a very long stretch of time, from the pre-pottery Neolithic until the middle of the twentieth century — **twelve thousand years** of continuity
- Compared to say, England (prosperous about five hundred years) or Scandinavia (less than three hundred years) — few areas on the planet have thrived with so much continuity over such a protracted stretch of time, what historians call **longue durée**
- Other cities came and went; Aleppo, Emesa (today Homs), and Laodicea (Lattakia) stayed relatively affluent
- The northern Levant was dominated by **traders**, largely owing to its position as a central spot on the **Silk Road**, and by agricultural lords supplying wheat to the Mediterranean world, particularly Rome
- The area supplied a few Roman emperors, a few Catholic popes before the schisms, and more than thirty **Greek language writers and philosophers** (including many heads of Plato's academy), in addition to the ancestors of **Steve Jobs**
- During Roman days the province was managed by **local elites** through a decentralized method of ruling that the Ottoman retained — cities minted their own coins
- After the Great War, one part of the northern Levant was integrated into the newly created nation of **Syria**, separated from its other section, now part of **Lebanon**
- The Ottoman type of imperial peace, the **pax Ottomana**, like its predecessor the **pax Romana**, was good for commerce — contracts were enforced, and that is what governments are needed for the most
- **Philip Mansel's** *Levant* documents how the cities of the Eastern Mediterranean operated as **city-states separated from the hinterland**
- A few decades into the life of Syria, the modernist **Baath Party** came to enforce utopias — as soon as the Baathists centralized the place and enforced their statist laws, Aleppo and Emesa went into **instant decline**
- The Baath Party's "modernization" program was to **remove the archaic mess of the souks** and replace them with the crisp modernism of the office building
- The effect was immediately visible: overnight the **trading families moved** to New York and New Jersey (for the Jews), California (for the Armenians), and Beirut (for the Christians)
- Beirut offered a commerce-friendly atmosphere, and **Lebanon was a benign, smaller, disorganized state** without any real central government — small enough to be a municipality on its own, smaller than a medium-size metropolitan area

### War, Prison, or Both

- While Lebanon had the right qualities, the state was **too loose** — by allowing Palestinian factions and Christian militias to own weapons, it caused an arms race between communities
- **Disorganized is invigorating; but the Lebanese state was one step too disorganized** — it would be like allowing New York mafia bosses to have a larger army than the Joint Chiefs of Staff (just imagine John Gotti with missiles)
- So in **1975** a raging civil war started in Lebanon
- A wealthy Aleppine merchant who fled the Baath regime, when asked by Taleb's grandfather why he did not return to Aleppo during the Lebanese war, answered categorically: **"We people of Aleppo prefer war to prison"** — by "prison" he meant the loss of political and economic freedoms
- Economic life, too, seems to prefer war to prison — Lebanon and Northern Syria had very similar wealth per individual about a century ago, identical cultures, languages, ethnicities, food, and even jokes
- Everything was the same except for the rule of the "modernizing" Baath Party in Syria compared to the totally benign state in Lebanon — in spite of a civil war that decimated the population, causing brain drain and setting wealth back by decades, today **Lebanon has a considerably higher standard of living — between three and six times the wealth of Syria**
- **Machiavelli** did not escape the point — Jean-Jacques Rousseau wrote, citing him:

> "It seemed, wrote Machiavelli, that in the midst of murders and civil wars, our republic became stronger [and] its citizens infused with virtues.... A little bit of agitation gives resources to souls and what makes the species prosper isn't peace, but freedom."

### Pax Romana

- The **centralized nation-state** is not exactly new in history — it existed in a nearly identical form in ancient Egypt, but it was an isolated event and did not survive for long; the Egyptian high state started collapsing upon contact with barbaric disorganized harassing invaders from Asia Minor with their assault chariots
- The dynasties of ancient Egypt did not run the place like an empire but like an **integrated state** — markedly different, as it produces different types of variations
- **Nation-states rely on centralized bureaucracy**; whereas empires like the Roman and Ottoman dynasties relied on local elites, allowing city-states to prosper and conserve effective **commercial autonomy**
- The Ottomans did vassals and suzerains a favor by preventing them from involvement in warfare — this took away militaristic temptations and helped them thrive, allowing locals to focus on commerce rather than war
- **David Hume** in his *History of England* argued in favor of small states, as large states get tempted by warfare
- Neither Romans nor Ottomans allowed local autonomy out of love of freedom — they did it for **convenience**. A combination of empire (for some affairs) and semi-independent regions (left alone for business) provides more stability than the centralized nation-state with flags and discrete borders
- Centralized states (Egypt, China) were in practice not too different except for the **centralization of intellect** with the scribes and the mandarinate system establishing a **monopoly of knowledge**
- Before modernity's communication networks (telegraph, train, telephone), a local provincial ruler was king for a large number of matters even though nominally subordinate — until recent history, the central state represented about **5 percent of the economy**, compared to about ten times that share in modern Europe
- Governments were sufficiently distracted by war to **leave economic affairs to businessmen**

### War or No War

- Before the nation-states of Germany and Italy ("re-unification"), Europe was a **fissiparous and amorphous mass** of small statelings and city-states in constant tension but shifting alliances
- Something comforting about statelings at war: mediocrity cannot handle more than one enemy, so war turns into an alliance — tension was always present somewhere but **without large consequences**, like mild rain and no floods. **Mediocristan**
- The contagious creation of nation-states in the late nineteenth century led to the two world wars and their sequels: **more than sixty million (and possibly eighty million) victims**
- The difference between war and *no war* became huge, with marked discontinuity — a switch to **"winner take all"** effects, the domination of rare events
- A collection of statelings is similar to the restaurant business: volatile, but you never have a generalized restaurant crisis, unlike the banking business — because it is composed of **independent and competing small units** that do not individually threaten the system. **Randomness is distributed rather than concentrated**
- Some people have fallen for the naive turkey-style belief that the world is getting safer and safer — they naively attribute safety to the holy "state" (though bottom-up Switzerland has about the lowest rate of violence)
- It is exactly like saying that **nuclear bombs are safer because they explode less often** — the world is subjected to fewer and fewer acts of violence, while wars have the potential to be more criminal
- We were very close to the **mother of all catastrophes in the 1960s** when the United States was about to pull the nuclear trigger on the Soviet Union
- In **Extremistan**, we don't look at evidence (evidence comes too late), we look at **potential damage** — never has the world been more prone to more damage; never. Risk is in the future, not in the past
- The messy multi-ethnic **Austro-Hungarian Empire** vanished after the Great War, along with its Ottoman neighbor and rival, replaced with crisp, clean nation-states
- The Ottoman Empire became the state of **Turkey, modeled after Switzerland**, with nobody noticing the inconsistency
- **Vienna** became trapped in Austria, sharing very little outside the formal language
- **Stefan Zweig**, the Viennese Jewish novelist then considered the most influential author in the world, expressed his pain in his poignant memoir *The World of Yesterday*
- Vienna joined the league of multicultural cities such as Alexandria, Aleppo, Prague, Thessaloniki, Constantinople (now Istanbul), Smyrna, and Trieste, now squeezed into the **Procrustean bed of the nation-state**, with citizens left in the grip of intergenerational nostalgia
- Unable to handle the loss and integrate elsewhere, Zweig later **committed suicide in Brazil** — Taleb first read his account while in a similar situation of physical and cultural exile when his Levantine Christian world was shattered by the Lebanese war

### Chapter 5 Footnotes (Key Points)

- A collection of small units with **semi-independent variations** produces vastly different risk characteristics than a single large unit (Henri Pirenne, Max Weber)
- The **European Union** is legally protected from overcentralization thanks to the **principle of subsidiarity**: things should be handled by the smallest possible unit that can manage them with efficacy — inherited from the Catholic Church
- When randomness gets distributed across a large number of small units with small recurrent political disorder, we get **Mediocristan**; when randomness concentrates, we get **Extremistan**
- People invoke "Balkanization" about the mess of fragmented states — but nobody uses **"Helvetization"** to describe its successes
- **Steven Pinker** misread the nature of the statistical process — a war that would decimate the planet would be completely consistent with the statistics; **Ben Bernanke** was similarly fooled with his *Great Moderation*, a turkey problem

---

## Chapter 6: Tell Them I Love (Some) Randomness

*Maxwell in Extremistan — Complicated mechanisms to feed a donkey — Virgil said to do it, and do it now*

### Tight Controls Cause Blowups (Maxwell's Governors)

- The point of the previous chapter was that the **risk properties of the fragile bank employee are vastly different from those of the comparatively antifragile artisan taxi driver** — likewise, the risk characteristic of a centralized system is different from that of a messy municipally-led confederation
- The second type is stable in the long run **because of** *some* dose of volatility
- A scientific argument showing how tight controls backfire and cause blowups was made by **James Clerk Maxwell** of electromagnetic theory fame
- **"Governors"** are contraptions meant to control the speed of steam engines by compensating for abrupt variations — they aimed at stabilizing engines, and apparently did, but paradoxically sometimes brought **capricious behavior and crashes**
- **Light control works; close control leads to overreaction**, sometimes causing the machinery to break into pieces
- In a famous paper **"On Governors"** (1867), Maxwell modeled the behavior and showed mathematically that **tightly controlling the speed of engines leads to instability**
- Maxwell's derivations can be generalized across domains to debunk **pseudo-stabilization and hidden long-term fragility**
- In markets, fixing prices or eliminating speculators (the so-called "noise traders") and the moderate volatility they bring provides an **illusion of stability**, with periods of calm punctuated with large jumps
- Because players are unused to volatility, the slightest price variation will be attributed to insider information or changes in the state of the system, causing **panics**
- When a currency never varies, a slight move makes people believe the world is ending — **injecting some confusion stabilizes the system**

### The Predictable Person

- Confusing people a little bit is beneficial — imagine someone extremely punctual who comes home at exactly six o'clock every day for fifteen years; you can use his arrival to set your watch. He will cause his family anxiety if he is **barely a few minutes late**
- Someone with a slightly more volatile schedule (half-hour variation) won't cause such alarm

### Variations as Purges

- **Small forest fires** periodically cleanse the system of the most flammable material — systematically preventing forest fires **"to be safe" makes the big one much worse**
- For similar reasons, **stability is not good for the economy**: firms become very weak during long periods of steady prosperity devoid of setbacks, and hidden vulnerabilities accumulate silently under the surface — delaying crises is not a good idea
- Absence of fluctuations in the market causes hidden risks to accumulate with impunity — the longer one goes without a market trauma, **the worse the damage when commotion occurs**
- Seasoned trading veterans used a heuristic: when a market reaches a **"new low"** (a level not seen in a long time), there is "a lot of blood" to come — people unused to losing shekels will incur great distress
- If such a low has not been seen in two years, it will cause **more damage than a one-year low** — tellingly, traders call it a "cleanup," getting the "weak hands" out of the way
- A **"weak hand"** is someone who is fragile but doesn't know it, lulled by a false sense of security — when many weak hands rush to the door, they collectively cause crashes
- A **volatile market** doesn't let people go such a long time without a "cleanup" of risks, thereby preventing such market collapses

> *Fluctuat nec mergitur* (fluctuates, or floats, but does not sink) — Latin saying

### Hungry Donkeys

- So far we have argued that preventing randomness in an antifragile system is not always a good idea — now consider the situation in which **adding randomness** has been a standard operating method, as fuel for an antifragile system permanently hungry for it
- **Buridan's Donkey**: A donkey equally famished and thirsty, caught at an equal distance between food and water, would unavoidably die — but he can be saved by a **random nudge** one way or the other
- Named after the medieval philosopher **Jean de Buridan** — when systems are stuck in a dangerous impasse, **randomness and only randomness can unlock them**. Absence of randomness equals guaranteed death
- **Stochastic resonance**: adding random noise to the background makes you hear sounds with **more accuracy** — not just a psychological effect of overcompensation, but a physical property of the system
- Weak SOS signals too weak to be picked up by remote receptors can become audible in the presence of background noise — by adding random hiss to the signal, it rises above the threshold of detection. **Nothing in that situation does better than randomness, which comes for free**
- **Annealing** in metallurgy: a technique to make metal stronger and more homogeneous through the heating and controlled cooling of a material — the heat causes atoms to become unstuck from initial positions and wander randomly through states of higher energy; the cooling gives them more chances of finding new, **better configurations**
- Taleb's father would tap a wooden **barometer** every day upon coming home — the stress got the needle unstuck and allowed it to find its true equilibrium position. A local brand of antifragility
- Inspired by the metallurgical technique, mathematicians use **simulated annealing** — a computer simulation method to bring more general optimal solutions that only randomness can deliver
