- Airlines figured out how to do it, but **not governments** when they estimate deficits
- This method is very general — Taleb even used it with **Fukushima-style computations** and realized how fragile their computation of small probabilities was — in fact **all small probabilities tend to be very fragile to errors**, as a small change in the assumptions can make the probability rise dramatically, from one per million to one per hundred — a **ten-thousand-fold underestimation**
- This method can show us where the math in economic models is bogus — which models are **fragile** and which are not — simply do a small change in the assumptions and look at how large the effect is; if there is **acceleration**, it implies that someone relying on the model blows up from Black Swan effects

> *Molto facile.*

- A detailed methodology to detect which results are bogus in economics — along with a discussion of small probabilities — is provided in the Appendix
- Much of what is taught in economics that has an equation, as well as econometrics, should be **immediately ditched** — which explains why economics is largely a **charlatanic profession**

> Fragilistas, *semper fragilisti*!

### How to Lose a Grandmother

- The following effect of nonlinearity: conditions under which **the average — the first order effect — does not matter**
- As the saying goes:

> *Do not cross a river if it is on average four feet deep.*

- You are informed that your grandmother will spend the next two hours at the very desirable average temperature of **seventy degrees Fahrenheit** (about 21°C) — excellent, you think, the optimal temperature for grandmothers
- But there is a second piece of data: your grandmother will spend the first hour at **zero degrees Fahrenheit** (around -18°C), and the second hour at **one hundred and forty degrees** (around 60°C) — for an average of the very desirable 70°F
- So you will most certainly end up with **no grandmother, a funeral, and possibly an inheritance**
- Temperature changes become **more and more harmful** as they deviate from seventy degrees — the variability turned out to be more important than the first piece of information (the average)
- The notion of average is of **no significance** when one is fragile to variations — the **dispersion** in possible thermal outcomes matters much more
- Your grandmother is fragile to **variations of temperature**, to the **volatility of the weather** — that second piece of information is the **second-order effect**, or more precisely, the **convexity effect**
- The notion of average can be a **Procrustean bed** — the information that the average temperature is 70°F does not simplify the situation for your grandmother; it is information squeezed into a Procrustean bed — and these are necessarily committed by scientific modelers, since a model is *by its very nature* a simplification
- You don't want the simplification to distort the situation to the point of being harmful

### Figure 16: Megafragility of Grandmother's Health

- **Figure 16** shows the fragility of the health of the grandmother to variations — health on the vertical axis, temperature on the horizontal — the shape **curves inward** — a "concave" shape, or **negative convexity effect**
- If the grandmother's response was "linear" (a straight line), then the harm of temperature below 70° would be offset by the benefits of temperature above it — but the fact is that health is **capped at a maximum**, otherwise she would keep improving
- General attributes of the grandmother's health response to temperature: (a) there is **nonlinearity** (the response is not a straight line), (b) it **curves inward** too much, and (c) the more nonlinear the response, the **less relevant the average**, and the more relevant the **stability around such average**

### Now the Philosopher's Stone

- Much of medieval thinking went into finding the **philosopher's stone** — chemistry is the child of alchemy, which consisted of looking into the chemical powers of substances
- The main efforts went into creating value by transforming metals into gold by the method of **transmutation** — the necessary substance was called the philosopher's stone (*lapis philosophorum*)
- Many people fell for it, including scholars like **Albertus Magnus, Isaac Newton, and Roger Bacon**, and great thinkers who were not quite scholars, such as **Paracelsus**
- The operation of transmutation was called the **Magnus Opus** — the great(est) work — Taleb truly believes that the operation he discusses — based on properties of optionality — is about as close as we can get to the philosopher's stone
- The following note helps understand: (a) the severity of the problem of **conflation** (mistaking the price of oil for geopolitics, or mistaking a profitable bet for good forecasting — not convexity of payoff and optionality), (b) why anything with **optionality** has a long-term advantage and how to measure it, and (c) an additional subtle property called **Jensen's inequality**

### The Traffic Example Revisited

- Recall the traffic example from Chapter 18: **90,000 cars** for an hour, then **110,000 cars** for the next one, for an average of 100,000, and traffic will be horrendous
- On the other hand, if we have **100,000 cars for both time periods**, traffic will be smooth and time in traffic short
- The number of cars is the **something** (a variable); traffic time is the **function of something** — the behavior of the function is such that the *function of something* becomes different from the *something* under nonlinearities
- Key principles of nonlinearity and volatility:
  - (a) The more **nonlinear**, the more the *function of something* divorces itself from the *something* — if traffic were linear, there would be no difference between 90,000/110,000 and 100,000/100,000
  - (b) The more **volatile** the something — the more uncertainty — the more the function divorces itself from the something — travel time depends more on the **volatility around the average** than the average itself — things degrade if there is unevenness of distribution
  - (c) If the function is **convex** (antifragile), then the average of the function of something is going to be **higher** than the function of the average of something — and the **reverse** when the function is concave (fragile)

### The Squaring Function Example (Convexity Bias)

- Assume the function is the **squaring function** (multiply a number by itself) — this is a convex function
- Take a conventional die (six sides) with a payoff equal to the number it lands on: 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, or 6
- The square of the expected (average) payoff is then (1+2+3+4+5+6 divided by 6)² = 3.5² = **12.25** — the **function of the average** equals 12.25
- But the average of the function is: (1²+2²+3²+4²+5²+6²) divided by 6 = **15.17** — the **average of the function** equals 15.17
- Since squaring is a **convex function**, the average of the square payoff is **higher** than the square of the average payoff
- The difference between 15.17 and 12.25 is what Taleb calls the **hidden benefit of antifragility** — here, a **24 percent "edge"**
- There are two biases: one elementary **convexity effect** (leading to mistaking the properties of the average of something, here 3.5, for those of the convex function of something, here 15.17), and the second, more involved, in **mistaking an average of a function for the function of an average** (here 15.17 for 12.25) — the latter represents **optionality**
- Someone with a **linear payoff** needs to be right more than 50 percent of the time; someone with a **convex payoff**, much less — the hidden benefit of antifragility is that you can **guess worse than random** and still end up outperforming

> *This explains my statement that you can be dumb and antifragile and still do very well.*

- This hidden "convexity bias" comes from a mathematical property called **Jensen's inequality** — this is what the common discourse on innovation is missing
- If you ignore the convexity bias, you are missing a chunk of what makes the nonlinear world go round — and it is a fact that such an idea is missing from the discourse

### How to Transform Gold into Mud: The Inverse Philosopher's Stone

- Take the same example using the **square root** function (concave, but much less concave than the square function is convex)
- The square root of the expected (average) payoff is √3.5 = **1.87** — the **function of the average** equals 1.87
- But the average of the function is: (√1+√2+√3+√4+√5+√6) divided by 6 = **1.80** — the **average of the function** equals 1.80
- The difference is called the **"negative convexity bias"** (or "concavity bias") — the hidden harm of fragility is that you need to be **much, much better than random** in your prediction and knowing where you are going, just to offset the negative effect

### Summary of Optionality and Convexity

- If you have **favorable asymmetries**, or **positive convexity** (options being a special case), then in the long run you will do reasonably well, **outperforming the average** in the presence of uncertainty
- The more uncertainty, the more role for optionality to kick in, and the more you will outperform — this property is very central to life

### Footnotes on Measurement and Model Risk

- The method of measuring convexity does not require a good model for risk measurement — you can use a ruler that you know is wrong; it can still tell you about rate of growth (acceleration) even if it can't measure absolute height
- **Convexity is about acceleration** — measuring convexity effects to detect model errors means that even if the model is wrong, it can tell you if an entity is fragile and by how much — we are only looking for **second-order effects**

---

## Book VI: Via Negativa

## Chapter 20: Via Negativa

### The Apophatic Method

- Recall that we had no name for the color blue but managed rather well without it — we stayed for a long part of our history **culturally, not biologically, color blind**
- Before the composition of Chapter 1, we did not have a name for **antifragility**, yet systems have relied on it effectively in the absence of human intervention
- There are many things without words, matters that we know and can act on but cannot describe directly — almost anything around us of significance is **hard to grasp linguistically** — and in fact the more powerful, the more incomplete our linguistic grasp
- If we cannot express what something is exactly, we can say something about what it is *not* — the indirect rather than the direct expression
- The **"apophatic"** focuses on what cannot be said directly in words, from the Greek *apophasis* (saying no, or mentioning without mentioning)
- The method began as an avoidance of direct description, leading to a focus on **negative description** — what is called in Latin **via negativa**, the negative way
- *Via negativa* does not try to express what God is — it just lists what God is **not** and proceeds by the process of **elimination**
- The idea is mostly associated with the mystical theologian **Pseudo-Dionysos the Areopagite** — he was some obscure Near Easterner by the name of Dionysos who wrote powerful mystical treatises and was confused with Dionysos the Areopagite, a judge in Athens converted by Paul the Apostle
- **Neoplatonists** were followers of Plato's ideas, focused on Plato's forms (abstract objects with distinct existence) — Pseudo-Dionysos was the disciple of **Proclus** the Neoplatonist (himself the student of Syrianus, another Syrian Neoplatonist)
- Proclus was known to repeat the metaphor that **statues are carved by subtraction**

> *Michelangelo was asked by the pope about the secret of his genius, particularly how he carved the statue of David. His answer was: "It's simple. I just remove everything that is not David."*

- The reader might recognize the logic behind the **barbell** — remember from the logic of the barbell that it is necessary to **first remove fragilities**

### Where Is the Charlatan?

- The **interventionista** focuses on positive action — *doing* — just like positive definitions, acts of commission are respected and glorified by our primitive minds
- Naive government interventions that end in disaster are followed by generalized complaints, which lead to more naive government interventions — acts of **omission** (*not* doing something) are not considered acts and do not appear to be part of one's mission
- Taleb's lifelong simple heuristic: **charlatans are recognizable** in that they will give you only positive advice, exploiting our gullibility and sucker-proneness for recipes that hit you in a flash as just obvious, then evaporate as you forget them
- Just look at the "how to" books with "Ten Steps for—" in their title (enrichment, weight loss, making friends, innovation, getting elected, building muscles, finding a husband, running an orphanage, etc.)
- Yet in practice it is the **negative** that's used by the pros, those selected by evolution: chess grandmasters usually win by **not losing**; people become rich by **not going bust**; religions are mostly about **interdicts**; the learning of life is about **what to avoid**
- You reduce most of your personal risks of accident thanks to a **small number of measures**
- Being fooled by randomness: in most circumstances fraught with a high degree of randomness, one cannot really tell if a successful person has skills — but we can pretty much predict the negative, that **a person totally devoid of skills will eventually fail**

### Subtractive Knowledge

- The greatest — and most robust — contribution to knowledge consists in **removing what we think is wrong** — **subtractive epistemology**
- In life, antifragility is reached by **not** being a sucker — in *Peri mystikes theologias*, Pseudo-Dionysos did not use these exact words, nor did he discuss disconfirmation, but in Taleb's view he figured out this subtractive epistemology and asymmetries in knowledge
- Taleb calls **"Platonicity"** the love of some crisp abstract forms, the theoretical forms and universals that make us blind to the mess of reality and cause Black Swan effects — then he realized there was an asymmetry: he truly believes in Platonic ideas when they come in **reverse**, like **negative universals**
- The central tenet of this epistemology: **we know a lot more what is wrong than what is right** — or, phrased according to the fragile/robust classification, **negative knowledge** (what is wrong, what does not work) is more robust to error than **positive knowledge** (what is right, what works)
- **Knowledge grows by subtraction** much more than by addition — what we know today might turn out to be wrong but what we know to be wrong cannot turn out to be right, at least not easily
- If I spot a black swan (not capitalized), I can be quite certain that "all swans are white" is wrong — but even if I have never seen a black swan, I can never hold such a statement to be true
- Since one small observation can disprove a statement, while millions can hardly confirm it, **disconfirmation is more rigorous than confirmation**
- This idea has been associated with philosopher **Karl Popper** — Taleb quite mistakenly thought he was its originator (though he is at the origin of the idea on the fundamental inability to predict the course of history)
- The notion is vastly more ancient — it was one of the central tenets of the **skeptical-empirical school of medicine** of the postclassical era in the Eastern Mediterranean, and well known to a group of nineteenth-century French scholars who rediscovered these works
- As you can see, we can link this to the general tableaus of **positive (additive)** and **negative (subtractive)**: negative knowledge is more robust
- **Popper's limitation**: he has been criticized for his treatment of disconfirmation as hard, unequivocal, black-and-white — it is not clear-cut whether an experiment failed to produce results because of "falsifying" the theory, the failure of tools, bad luck, or fraud by the scientist
- But he missed that if an incompetent surgeon is operating on a brain, one can safely predict serious damage — yet such subtractive representation of the future is perfectly in line with his idea of disconfirmation
- **Popper's masterpiece**, *The Poverty of Historicism*, presents the limits of forecasting and shows the impossibility of an acceptable representation of the future — but he missed the point that if an incompetent surgeon is operating on a brain, one can safely predict serious damage, even the death of the patient — what he calls falsification of a theory should lead, in practice, to the **breaking of the object** of its application

### Via Negativa in Political Systems

- In political systems, a good mechanism is one that helps **remove the bad guy** — it's not about what to do or who to put in — for the bad guy can cause more harm than the collective actions of good ones
- **Jon Elster** recently wrote a book with the telling title *Preventing Mischief*, basing negative action on **Bentham's idea** that "the art of the legislator is limited to the prevention of everything that might prevent the development of their [members of the assembly] liberty and their intelligence"
- *Via negativa* is part of classical wisdom — for the Arab scholar and religious leader **Ali Bin Abi-Taleb** (no relation), keeping one's distance from an ignorant person is equivalent to keeping company with a wise man

> *"People think focus means saying yes to the thing you've got to focus on. But that's not what it means at all. It means saying no to the hundred other good ideas that there are. You have to pick carefully. I'm actually as proud of the things we haven't done as the things I have done. Innovation is saying no to 1,000 things."* — Steve Jobs

### Barbells, Again

- **Subtractive knowledge** is a form of barbell — critically, it is **convex**: what is wrong is quite robust; what you don't know is fragile and speculative, but you do not take it seriously so you make sure it does not harm you in case it turns out to be false
- Another application of *via negativa* lies in the **less-is-more** idea

### Less Is More

- The less-is-more idea in decision making can be traced to **Spyros Makridakis, Robyn Dawes, Dan Goldstein, and Gerd Gigerenzer**, who have all found that **simpler methods** for forecasting and inference can work much, much better than complicated ones
- Their simple rules of thumb are not perfect, but are designed to not be perfect — adopting some intellectual humility and abandoning the aim at sophistication can yield powerful effects
- **Goldstein and Gigerenzer** coined the notion of **"fast and frugal" heuristics** that make good decisions despite limited time, knowledge, and computing power
- The less-is-more heuristic fell into Taleb's work in two places: (1) in domains of **extreme effects** where the rare event plays a disproportionate share — just worry about **Black Swan exposures** and life is easy; (2) *less is more* has proved shockingly easy to find and apply — and "robust" to mistakes and change of minds
- There may not be an easily identifiable cause for a large share of the problems, but often there is an easy solution (good enough) — sometimes identifiable with the **naked eye** rather than complicated analyses

### The Eighty/Twenty Rule and Extremistan Effects

- The **eighty/twenty idea** (based on **Vilfredo Pareto's** discovery more than a century ago): 20 percent of the people in Italy owned 80 percent of the land, and vice versa — of these 20 percent, 4 percent would have owned around 64 percent; less than 1 percent representing about 50 percent of the total
- These describe **winner-take-all Extremistan effects** — very general, from the distribution of wealth to book sales per author
- We are moving into the far more uneven distribution of **99/1** across many things that used to be 80/20: 99 percent of Internet traffic is attributable to less than 1 percent of sites, 99 percent of book sales come from less than 1 percent of authors
- Almost everything contemporary has **winner-take-all effects**, which includes sources of harm and benefits — accordingly, **1 percent modification** of systems can lower fragility (or increase antifragility) by about **99 percent** — all it takes is a few steps, very few steps, often at low cost
- Examples: a small number of **homeless people** cost the states a disproportionate share of the bills; a small number of **employees** in a corporation cause the most problems; a small number of **customers** generate a large share of the revenues
- **Ezekiel Emanuel** showed that half the population accounts for less than 3 percent of health care costs, with the sickest 10 percent consuming 64 percent of the total pie
- **Bent Flyvbjerg** showed in his *Black Swan management* idea that the bulk of cost overruns by corporations are simply attributable to large technology projects

> *As they say in the mafia, just work on removing the pebble in your shoe.*

- In some domains like **real estate**, problems and solutions are crisply summarized by a heuristic: **"location, location, and location"** — much of the rest takes care of itself

### More Data, Less Predictability

- Yet people want more data to "solve problems" — Taleb testified in Congress against a project to fund a crisis forecasting project — we have **never had more data** than we have now, yet have **less predictability than ever**
- More data — such as paying attention to the eye colors of people crossing the street — can make you miss the **big truck**
- When you cross the street, you remove data, anything but the essential threat

> *As Paul Valéry once wrote: que de choses il faut ignorer pour agir — how many things one should disregard in order to act.*

- **Convincing and confident disciplines** (physics) tend to use little statistical backup, while **political science and economics**, which have never produced anything of note, are full of elaborate statistics and statistical "evidence"
- The situation in science is similar to detective novels: the person with the **largest number of alibis** turns out to be the guilty one
- You do not need reams of paper full of data to destroy the megatons of papers using statistics in economics: the simple argument that **Black Swans and tail events run the socioeconomic world** — and these events cannot be predicted — is sufficient to invalidate their statistics

### The Invisible Gorilla and Decision Making

- **Christopher Chabris and Daniel Simons**, in their book *The Invisible Gorilla*, show how people watching a video of a basketball game, when diverted with attention-absorbing details such as counting passes, can completely **miss a gorilla stepping into the middle of the court**
- Taleb had been intuitively using the less-is-more idea as an aid in decision making (contrary to putting pros and cons side by side on a computer screen)
- If you have **more than one reason** to do something (choose a doctor, hire a gardener, marry a person, go on a trip), **just don't do it** — it does not mean that one reason is better than two, just that by invoking more than one reason you are trying to **convince yourself** to do something
- **Obvious decisions** (robust to error) require no more than a **single reason**
- The French army had a heuristic to reject excuses for absenteeism for **more than one reason** (like death of grandmother, cold virus, and being bitten by a boar)
- If someone attacks a book or idea using **more than one argument**, you know it is not real — nobody says "he is a criminal, he killed many people, and he also has bad table manners and bad breath and is a very poor driver"

### Bergson's Razor

- Taleb often follows what he calls **Bergson's razor**: "A philosopher should be known for one single idea, not more"
- **Paul Valéry** once asked Einstein if he carried a notebook to write down ideas — "I never have ideas" was the reply (he just did not have trivial ideas)
- Heuristic: if someone has a **long bio**, skip him — if someone has a résumé that "can cover more than two or three lives," skip him
- When told that someone has three hundred academic papers and twenty-two honorary doctorates, but no other single compelling contribution or main idea behind it, Taleb avoids him like **the bubonic plague**

---

## Chapter 20: Time and Fragility

> *Prophecy, like knowledge, is subtractive, not additive — The Lindy effect, or how the old prevails over the new, especially in technology, no matter what they say in California — Prophecy not a recommended and voluntary career*

### The Old Is Superior to the New

- Antifragility implies — contrary to initial instinct — that **the old is superior to the new**, and much more than you think
- No matter how something looks to your intellectual machinery, or how well or poorly it narrates, **time will know more about its fragilities** and break it when necessary
- Taleb exposes a contemporary disease linked to interventionism called **neomania**, which brings fragility but he believes may be treatable if one is patient enough
- What survives must be good at serving some (mostly hidden) purpose that time can see but our eyes and logical faculties can't capture
- In this chapter, the notion of **fragility** is used as a central driver of prediction
- Recall the foundational asymmetry: the **antifragile benefits** from volatility and disorder, the **fragile is harmed** — well, **time is the same as disorder**

### From Simonides to Jensen

- As an exercise in the use of the distinction between fragility and antifragility, let us play prophet — it is **not a good career choice** unless you have a thick skin, a good circle of friends, little access to the Internet, a library with ancient proverbs, and the ability to derive personal benefits from your prophecy
- As shown from the track record of the prophets: before you are proven right, you will be **reviled**; after you are proven right, you will be **hated** for a while, or worse, your ideas will appear to be "trivial" thanks to **retrospective distortion**
- This makes it far more convincing to follow the **Fat Tony method** of focusing on shekels more than recognition
- Twentieth-century intellectuals who embraced the wrong ideas (Communism, Stalinism) remained **fashionable** — while those like political philosopher **Raymond Aron**, who saw the problems, got short shrift both before and after being acknowledged as right
- Close your eyes and imagine your future surroundings in five, ten, or twenty-five years — odds are your imagination will produce *new* things: **innovation, improvements, killer technologies** — these common concepts are nonsense both **empirically and philosophically**
- Why? Your imagination will be **adding things** to the present world — but the correct approach according to the notions of fragility and antifragility is to **take away** from the future, reduce from it, simply subtract things that do not belong to the coming times
- **Via negativa**: what is fragile will eventually break; and luckily, we can easily tell what is fragile — **positive Black Swans** are more unpredictable than negative ones

> *"Time has sharp teeth that destroy everything,"* declared the sixth-century (B.C.) poet Simonides of Ceos.

- From Ovid (*tempus edax rerum* — time devours everything) to Franco-Russian poetess **Elsa Triolet** ("time burns but leaves no ashes")
- Thanks to convexity effects, we can put a little bit of science in these and produce our own taxonomy: **the fragile will be devoured the fastest** by inexorable time
- The fragile will eventually break — and we are capable of figuring out what is fragile — even what we believe is antifragile will eventually break, but it should take much, **much longer** to do so (wine does well with time, but up to a point; and not if you put it in the crater of a volcano)
- The verse by Simonides continues with the stipulation **"even the most solid"** — he had the adumbration that the most solid will be swallowed with more difficulty, hence last — naturally, he did not think that something could be antifragile, hence never swallowed
- The **via negativa method of prophecy** is the only valid one: there is no other way to produce a forecast without being a turkey — in the complex environment in which we live, new technologies will emerge but what is currently fragile **will be replaced** by something else — but this "something else" is unpredictable
- The most fragile is the **predictive** — in other words, those who underestimate Black Swans will eventually exit the population
- **Longer-term predictions are more reliable** than short-term ones (an apparent paradox): one can be quite certain that what is Black Swan-prone will be eventually swallowed by history since time augments the probability — on the other hand, typical predictions (not involving the currently fragile) **degrade with time**; in the presence of nonlinearities, the longer the forecast the worse its accuracy

### Learning to Subtract

- Consider the **futuristic projections** made throughout the past century and a half in literary novels (Jules Verne, H. G. Wells, George Orwell) or by scientists/futurists — the tools that currently dominate the world (the Internet, the wheel on the suitcase) were **completely missing** from these forecasts
- The major error is not here: almost everything that was imagined **never took place**, except for a few overexploited anecdotes (Hero the Alexandrian's steam engine, Leonardo da Vinci's assault vehicle)
- Our world looks too close to theirs, much closer than they ever imagined — and we tend to be blind to that fact — there seems to be **no correcting mechanism** for forecasting a highly technocratic future
- There may be a selection bias: those who engage in producing these accounts of the future will tend to have (incurable and untreatable) **neomania**, the love of the modern for its own sake

### The Dinner at the Restaurant (Technology Constancy)

- Tonight Taleb will meet friends in a **restaurant** (tavernas have existed for at least twenty-five centuries), wearing **shoes** hardly different from those worn fifty-three hundred years ago by the mummified man discovered in the Austrian Alps
- At the restaurant: **silverware** (a Mesopotamian technology), **wine** (in use for at least six millennia), **glasses** (an innovation claimed by Lebanese compatriots, glass objects sold as trinkets for at least twenty-nine hundred years), **artisanal cheese** (unchanged for several centuries), food prepared using **fire** and kitchen implements unchanged since the Romans
- He will be sitting on a **(at least) three-thousand-year-old** device: the **chair** — and will not be repairing to the restaurant with a flying motorcycle, but walking or taking a **cab** from a century-old technology, driven by an immigrant
- **David Edgerton** showed that in the early 2000s we produce two and a half times as many **bicycles** as cars and invest most of our technological resources in **maintaining existing equipment** or refining old technologies
- One of the most consequential technologies seems to be the one people talk about the least: the **condom** — it has been undergoing meaningful improvements, with the precise aim of being less and less noticeable

### The Prime Error in Imagining the Future

- The **prime error** when asked to imagine the future: we take the present as a baseline, then produce a speculative destiny by adding new technologies and products to it, interpolating from past developments
- We represent society according to our **utopia of the moment**, largely driven by our wishes — except for a few doomsayers, the future will be largely inhabited by our desires
- We tend to **over-technologize** the future and underestimate the might of the equivalent of small wheels on suitcases that will be staring at us for the next millennia
- Cooking utensils from **Pompeii** are hardly different from those found in today's good kitchens — a powerful illustration of technological constancy

### The Blindness of Technothinkers

- After leaving finance, Taleb attended fashionable technology conferences attended by pre-rich, post-rich technology people and the new category of **technology intellectuals**
- These conferences, while colorful and slick with computerized images and fancy animations, felt **depressing** — it was not just their additive approach to the future (failure to subtract the fragile rather than add to destiny)
- It was their blindness by **uncompromising neomania** and a profound **lack of elegance**
- Technothinkers tend to have an **"engineering mind"** — to put it less politely, they have autistic tendencies: lack of charm, interest in objects instead of persons, neglect of their looks, precision at the expense of applicability, and an absence of **literary culture**
- The absence of literary culture is actually a **marker of future blindness** because it is usually accompanied by a denigration of history, a byproduct of unconditional neomania
- Outside of science fiction, **literature is about the past** — we do not learn physics or biology from medieval textbooks, but we still read Homer, Plato, or the very modern Shakespeare
- We cannot talk about sculpture without knowledge of Phidias, Michelangelo, or the great **Canova** — these are in the past, not in the future
- By setting foot into a **museum**, the aesthetically minded person is connecting with the elders — the past, properly handled, is a much better teacher about the properties of the future than the present
- To understand the future, you do not need technoautistic jargon or obsession with "killer apps" — you need: respect for the past, curiosity about the historical record, a hunger for the wisdom of the elders, and a grasp of **"heuristics"** — the often unwritten rules of thumb determining survival

### Technology at Its Best

- Technology can cancel the effect of bad technologies by **self-subtraction**

> Technology is at its best when it is invisible.

- Technology is of greatest benefit when it **displaces the deleterious**, unnatural, alienating, and inherently fragile preceding technology
- Many modern applications that have survived today came to disrupt the **deleterious effects of the philistinism of modernity**, particularly the twentieth century: the large multinational bureaucratic corporation with "empty suits" at the top; the isolated nuclear family with the television set; car-designed suburban society; the militaristic nation-state with border controls; the dictatorship on thought and culture by established media; large corporations controlling markets (now threatened by the Internet); pseudorigor ("press 1 for English")
- The Internet removed many **unnatural elements** around us — the absence of paperwork makes bureaucracy more palatable; a computer virus will wipe out all records and free people from their past mistakes

### Reversing Technology (Barefoot Shoes)

- We are now using technology to **reverse technology** — the shoe industry, after spending decades "engineering" the perfect walking and running shoe with "support" mechanisms and material for cushioning, is now selling shoes that replicate being **barefoot**
- These unobtrusive shoes' only claimed function is to **protect our feet from the elements**, not to dictate how we walk as the modernistic mission was — they are selling us the calloused feet of a hunter-gatherer
- Walking in nature with these shoes feels like waking up to a **new dimension** while feeling the three dimensions of the terrain — regular shoes feel like casts that separate us from the environment
- The technology is in the **sole, not the shoe** — the new soles can be both robust and very thin, allowing the foot to hug the ground as if one were barefoot
- Taleb's best discovery is an Italian-looking **moccasin made in Brazil** that allows him to both run on stones and go to dinner in restaurants
- Perhaps they should just sell us **reinforced waterproof socks** (in effect, what the Alpine fellow had), but it would not be very profitable

### The Tablet as Return to Roots

- The great use of the **tablet computer** (notably the iPad) is that it allows us to return to Babylonian and Phoenician roots of writing and taking notes on a tablet (which is how it started)
- One can now jot down handwritten, or rather fingerwritten, notes — it is much more soothing to **write longhand** instead of having to go through the agency of a keyboard
- It may be a natural property of technology to **only want to be displaced by itself**

## Chapter 20: The Lindy Effect

### The Lindy Effect Defined

- Time to get more technical — a distinction is helpful: separate the **perishable** (humans, single items) from the **nonperishable**, the potentially perennial
- The **nonperishable** is anything that does not have an organic unavoidable expiration date — the perishable is typically an object; the nonperishable has an **informational nature** to it
- A single car is perishable, but the **automobile as a technology** has survived about a century (and we can speculate should survive another one)
- Humans die, but their **genes** — a code — do not necessarily; the physical book is perishable — say, a specific copy of the Old Testament — but its **contents** are not, as they can be expressed into another physical book
- The **Lebanese dialect** illustration: when you see a young and an old human, you can be confident that the younger will survive the elder; with something nonperishable like a technology, that is **not the case**
- Two possibilities: either both are expected to have the same additional life expectancy (the case of **exponential** probability distribution), or the old is expected to have a **longer** expectancy than the young, in proportion to their relative age

### Table 6: Domains and Comparison of Life Expectancy

- **Perishable** (life of humans and other animals): Gaussian distribution — the young is expected to live longer than the old
- **Non-perishable informational** (lifetime of species): Exponential — both the young and old have equivalent life expectancy
- **Lindy Effect** (non-perishable informational: life of intellectual production, lifetime of genera): **Power law** — the old is expected to stay longer than the young in proportion to their age

### The Core Lindy Principle

- Building on the so-called Lindy effect in the version later developed by **Benoît Mandelbrot**:

> *For the perishable, every additional day in its life translates into a shorter additional life expectancy. For the nonperishable, every additional day may imply a longer life expectancy.*

- The longer a technology lives, the longer it can be expected to live
- Example: a man who is **40 years old** — actuarial tables predict he has an extra 44 years to go; next year at 41, he will have a little more than 43 years to go — every year that elapses **reduces** his life expectancy by about a year (a little less than a year)
- The **opposite** applies to nonperishable items: if a book has been in print for **forty years**, expect it to be in print for another forty years; if it survives another decade, then it will be expected to be in print another **fifty years**
- Things that have been around for a long time are not "aging" like persons, but **"aging" in reverse** — every year that passes without extinction **doubles** the additional life expectancy
- The **robustness** of an item is proportional to its life!

### Richard Gott and Broadway Shows

- Physicist **Richard Gott** applied completely different reasoning to state that whatever we observe in a randomly selected way is likely to be neither in the beginning nor in the end of its life, most likely in its **middle**
- His argument was criticized for being incomplete, but by testing it he tested the principle that the expected life of an item is proportional to its past life
- Gott made a list of **Broadway shows** on May 17, 1993 and predicted that the longest-running ones would last longest, and vice versa — he was proven right with **95 percent accuracy**
- As a child, he had visited both the **Great Pyramid** (fifty-seven hundred years old) and the **Berlin Wall** (twelve years old), and correctly guessed the former would outlive the latter
- The proportionality of life expectancy does not need to be tested explicitly — it is the direct result of **"winner-take-all" effects** in longevity

### Two Common Mistakes About the Lindy Effect

- **Mistake 1: The counterexample fallacy** — people present counterexamples of technology currently seen as inefficient and dying (telephone land lines, print newspapers, paper receipts for tax purposes) and get angry
- Taleb's argument is not about *every* technology but about **life expectancy**, which is simply a probabilistically derived average
- Analogy: if a forty-year-old has terminal pancreatic cancer, one no longer estimates his life expectancy using unconditional insurance tables — likewise, the World Wide Web being less than about twenty years old will *only* have another twenty to go as a **noisy estimator** that should work on average, not in every case
- In general, the older the technology, not only the longer it is expected to last, but the **more certainty** one can attach to such a statement

> Remember the following principle: I am not saying that *all* technologies do not age, only that those technologies that were prone to aging are already dead.

- **Mistake 2: The "young" technology fallacy** — believing one would be acting "young" by adopting a "young" technology, revealing both a logical error and mental bias
- It leads to the **inversion of the power of generational contributions**, producing the illusion of the contribution of the new generations over the old — statistically, the "young" do almost nothing
- Loving things historical is not acting "old" — by loving the classics ("older"), one would be acting **"older" than** if interested in "younger" medieval themes
- A technology, being informational rather than physical, **does not age organically** like humans — the wheel is not "old" in the sense of experiencing degeneracy

### The Young and Progress

- The idea of "young" and "old" attached to certain crowd behavior is even more dangerous — if those who don't watch prepackaged 18-minute hyped-up lectures on the Web paid attention to people in their teens and twenties, they would be thinking differently
- Much progress comes from the young because of their relative **freedom from the system** and courage to take action that older people lose as they become trapped in life
- It is precisely the young who propose ideas that are **fragile** — not because they are young, but because most **unseasoned ideas are fragile**
- Someone who sells "futuristic" ideas will not make a lot of money selling the value of the past — **new technology is easier to hype up**

### Teaching Children for the Future

- Paul Doolan from Zurich asked how to teach children skills for the twenty-first century since we do not know which skills will be needed
- Taleb's answer, an elegant application of Karl Popper's error of historicism: make them **read the classics** — the future is in the past

> *He who does not have a past has no future.* — Arabic proverb

### A Few Mental Biases

- Application of the **fooled by randomness** effect: information has a nasty property — it hides failures
- Many people are drawn to financial markets after hearing success stories of someone getting rich in the stock market — but since failures are buried, investors are led to **overestimate their chances of success**
- The same applies to novels: we do not see the wonderful novels that are completely out of print — we confuse the **necessary and the causal**: because all surviving technologies have some obvious benefits, we are led to believe all technologies offering obvious benefits will survive
- Another mental bias: the overhyping of technology comes from the fact that we notice **change, not statics** — the classic example discovered by **Daniel Kahneman and Amos Tversky** applies to wealth
- If you announce to someone "you lost $10,000," he will be much more upset than if you tell him "your portfolio value, which was $785,000, is now $775,000" — our brains have a predilection for **shortcuts**, and the variation is easier to notice (and store) than the entire record
- This psychological heuristic, the error of **variation in place of total**, is quite pervasive even with matters that are visual
- We rely more on **water** than on cell phones, but because water does not change and cell phones do, we are prone to thinking that cell phones play a larger role than they do
- Because new generations are more aggressive with technology, we notice that they try more things, but we ignore that these implementations **don't usually stick** — most "innovations" are failures, just as most books are flops

### Neomania and Treadmill Effects

- Driving on the highway in your two-year-old Japanese car, you are overtaken by the latest version that looks markedly different — and markedly better? **Markedly better?**
- The bumper is slightly larger, the taillights wider — other than cosmetic details (and perhaps hidden technical improvements) representing less than a few percentage points in variation, the car **looks the same**
- You just see the lights and feel you are due an **upgrade** — costing about a third of the price of a new vehicle, all motivated by small, mostly cosmetic variations
- Switching cars is a small cost compared to switching **computers** — the recovery value of an old computer is negligible
- With Apple Mac computers: you just bought a new version, the person on the plane next to you has an older version that has a family resemblance but looks inferior — you forget the days when you had the same model and were **thrilled** with it
- With so many technologically driven and modernistic items, we notice **differences between versions** rather than commonalities — we rapidly tire of what we have, continuously searching for versions 2.0 and similar iterations
- These impulses to buy new things that will eventually lose their novelty are called **treadmill effects** — studied by Danny Kahneman and his peers as the psychology of **hedonic states**
- People acquire a new item, feel *more satisfied* after an initial boost, then rapidly **revert to their baseline** of well-being — when you "upgrade," you feel a boost of satisfaction with *changes* in technology, then get used to it and start hunting for the *new* new thing

### The Artisanal vs. the Technological

- We don't incur the same treadmilling techno-dissatisfaction with **classical art, older furniture** — whatever we do not put in the category of the technological
- You may have an oil painting and a flat-screen TV in the same room — the oil painting is an imitation of a classic Flemish scene; you are not eager to upgrade the painting but certain that your **flat-screen TV will be donated** to the local chapter of some kidney foundation
- Same with **dishes** — recall that we try to replicate nineteenth-century dinner customs; there is at least one domain in which we do not try to optimize matters
- Taleb writes longhand using a **seasoned fountain pen** — many of his pens are old enough to cross decades; he prefers **Clairefontaine paper and notebooks** that have hardly changed since his early childhood
- But when transcribing his writing into electronic form, he worries that his Mac computer may not be the best tool and plans to upgrade

### The On-Off Switch Heuristic

- There may be a heuristic that helps put items in categories: the **electronic on-off switch** — whatever has an "off" or "on" switch that needs to be turned off before being yelled at by the flight attendant will necessarily be in one category (but not the opposite: not as many items without an on-off switch will be prone to neomania)
- For items without an on-off switch, focus on **variations** with attendant neomania
- Consider the difference between the **artisanal** and the **industrial** — what is artisanal has the love of the maker infused in it, and tends to satisfy; we don't have this nagging impression of incompleteness we encounter with electronics
- Whatever is **technological** happens to be fragile — articles made by an artisan cause fewer treadmill effects; they tend to have some **antifragility** (recall how artisanal shoes take months before becoming comfortable)
- Items with an **on-off switch** tend to have no such redeeming antifragility

### Architecture and the Irreversible Neomania

- There is evolutionary warfare between architects producing a compounded form of **neomania**
- The problem with modernistic — and functional — architecture is that it is **not fragile enough to break physically**, so these buildings stick out just to torture our consciousness — you cannot exercise your prophetic powers by leaning on their fragility

### Top-Down vs. Bottom-Up in Urban Planning

- Urban planning demonstrates the central property of the **top-down effect**: top-down is usually irreversible, so mistakes tend to stick; whereas bottom-up is **gradual and incremental**, with creation and destruction along the way, though presumably with a positive slope
- Things that grow in a natural way, whether cities or individual houses, have a **fractal quality** to them — like everything alive, all organisms (lungs, trees) grow in some form of self-guided but tame randomness
- **Fractal** (Mandelbrot's insight): entails both jaggedness and a form of self-similarity ("self-affinity"), such as trees spreading into branches that look like small trees, and smaller branches that look like a slightly modified but recognizable version of the whole
- Everything in nature is fractal, jagged, and rich in detail — the **smooth**, by comparison, belongs to Euclidian geometry we study in school, simplified shapes that lose this layer of wealth
- Contemporary architecture is smooth, even when it tries to look whimsical — what is **top-down is generally unwrinkled** (unfractal) and feels dead

### Gaudi and Naturalistic Architecture

- Sometimes modernism can take a **naturalistic turn**, then stop in its tracks — **Gaudi's buildings** in Barcelona, from around the turn of the twentieth century, are inspired by nature and rich architecture (Baroque and Moorish)
- A rent-controlled apartment in a Gaudi building felt like an improved cavern with **rich, jagged details** — wealth of details, ironically, leads to inner peace
- Yet Gaudi's idea went nowhere except in promoting modernism in its unnatural and naive versions: later modernistic structures are smooth and **completely stripped of fractal jaggedness**
- White walls with sharp corners and **Euclidian angles** and crisp shapes strain us — once built, there is no way to get rid of them; almost everything built since World War II has an **unnatural smoothness** to it

### The Harm of Modernistic Buildings

- For some, these buildings cause more than aesthetic harm — many **Romanians** are bitter about Ceausescu's destruction of traditional villages replaced by modern high-rises
- **Neomania and dictatorship** are an explosive combination — in France, some blame the modernistic architecture of housing projects for the immigrant riots
- Christopher Caldwell wrote about the unnatural living conditions: **"Le Corbusier** called houses 'machines for living.' France's housing projects became machines for alienation."

### Jane Jacobs and the City as Organism

- **Jane Jacobs**, the New York urban activist, took a heroic stance as a political-style resistant against neomania in architecture and urban planning
- The modernistic dream was carried by **Robert Moses**, who wanted to improve New York by razing tenements and installing large roads and highways — committing a greater crime against natural order than **Haussmann**, who removed entire neighborhoods of Paris for the "Grand Boulevards"
- Jacobs stood against tall buildings as they deform the experience of urban living, which is conducted at **street level** — her bone with Robert Moses: highways and engines for travel suck life out of the city — a city should be devoted to **pedestrians**
- The **machine-organism dichotomy**: to Jacobs the city is an organism; for Moses it is a machine to be improved upon
- Moses had plans to raze the **West Village**; thanks to Jacobs' petitions and unremitting resistance, the neighborhood — the prettiest in Manhattan — has survived nearly intact
- Some of Moses' projects were beneficial, such as the **parks and beaches** now accessible to the middle class thanks to the highways

### London's Traffic and the Appeal of Old Cities

- The speed of traveling in London is equal to what it was a century and a half ago, if not slower — the same principle about municipal properties applies: neighborhoods are villages and **need to remain villages**
- London has so much traffic because people **wanted to be there** — the costs were exceeded by the benefits; more than a third of residents are foreign-born, and most high net worth individuals get their starter pied-à-terre in Central London
- The **absence of large avenues** and absence of a dominating state is part of its appeal — nobody would buy a pied-à-terre in **Brasilia**, the perfectly top-down city built from scratch on a map
- The most expensive neighborhoods in Paris today (the Sixth Arrondissement or Île Saint-Louis) were the ones **left alone** by nineteenth-century renovators

### Buildings Evolve

- The best argument against **teleological design**: even after they are built, buildings keep incurring mutations as if they needed to slowly evolve and be taken over by the dynamical environment — they change colors, shapes, windows, and character
- In his book *How Buildings Learn*, **Stewart Brand** shows in pictures how buildings change through time, as if they needed to metamorphose into unrecognizable shapes — strangely, buildings when erected do not account for the **optionality of future alterations**

### Wall to Wall Windows

- The skepticism about architectural modernism is not unconditional — some elements are an improvement: **floor-to-ceiling windows** in a rural environment expose us to nature, here again technology making itself (literally) invisible
- In the past, window size was dictated by **thermal considerations** (heat escaped quickly from windows); much French architecture was also a response to the **tax on windows** installed after the Revolution
- Modern technology allows us to **reverse from the smooth stone to the rich fractal and natural** — Benoît Mandelbrot wrote in front of a window overlooking trees, craving fractal aesthetics so much that the alternative would have been inconceivable
- Now an entire wall can be **transparent** and face lush and densely forested areas — as expressed by Oswald Spengler, civilization goes from plants to stone, from the fractal to the Euclidian; we are now moving back

### Metrification

- One example of the neomania of states: the campaign for **metrification** — the use of the metric system to replace "archaic" ones on grounds of efficiency
- **Warwick Cairns**, a fellow similar to Jane Jacobs, has been fighting in courts to let market farmers in Britain keep selling bananas by the **pound** and resist the use of the more "rational" kilogram
- The idea of metrification was born out of the **French Revolution**, as part of the utopian mood: changing the names of winter months to *Nivôse, Pluviôse, Ventôse*; having decimal time, ten-day weeks — the project of changing time has failed, but the metric system was implemented
- The old system has remained **refractory** in the United States and England
- French writer **Edmond About** visited Greece in 1832 and reports how peasants struggled with the metric system as completely unnatural and stuck to **Ottoman standards** instead
- The "modernization" of the Arabic alphabet from the easy-to-memorize old Semitic sequence (ABJAD, HAWWAZ) to the logical sequence A-B-T-TH has created a generation of Arabic speakers **without the ability to recite their alphabet**

### The Logic of Traditional Measurements

- Naturally born weights and measures have a **logic** to them: we use feet, miles, pounds, inches, furlongs, stones because these are remarkably **intuitive** and can be used with minimal expenditure of cognitive effort
- All cultures seem to have similar measurements with some physical correspondence to the everyday: a **meter** does not match anything; a **foot** does — "thirty feet" has meaning; a **mile** (from Latin *milia passum*) is a thousand paces; a **stone** (14 pounds) corresponds to... well, a stone; an **inch** (or *pouce*) corresponds to a thumb; a **furlong** is the distance one can sprint before running out of breath; a **pound** (from *libra*) is what you can imagine holding in your hands
- The *thekel* or *shekel* in Canaanite-Semitic languages means "weight" — something with a physical connotation, similar to the pound; the **digital system** itself comes from the correspondence to the ten fingers

## Chapter 20: Turning Science into Journalism

### Time as a Filter for Information

- We can apply criteria of fragility and robustness to the handling of **information** — the fragile in that context is, like technology, what does not stand the test of time
- The best filtering heuristic consists in taking into account the **age of books and scientific papers**
- Books that are one year old are usually not worth reading (a very low probability of having the qualities for "surviving"), no matter the hype and how "earth-shattering" they may seem
- Taleb follows the **Lindy effect** as a guide in selecting what to read: books that have been around for ten years will be around for ten more; books around for two millennia should be around for quite a bit of time
- Many understand this but do not apply it to **academic work**, which is in much of its modern practice hardly different from journalism (except for the occasional original production)
- Academic work, because of its **attention-seeking orientation**, can be easily subjected to Lindy effects: the hundreds of thousands of papers that are just noise, in spite of how hyped they were at publication

### Scientific Breakthroughs vs. Noise

- The problem in deciding whether a scientific result or a new "innovation" is a breakthrough (the opposite of noise) is that one needs to see **all aspects** of the idea — and there is always some opacity that time, and only time, can dissipate
- Example: the work of **Judah Folkman**, who believed one could cure cancer by choking the blood supply (tumors require nutrition and tend to create new blood vessels, called **neovascularization**) — the idea looked impeccable on paper, but about a decade and a half later, the only significant result was completely outside cancer, in the mitigation of **macular degeneration**
- Likewise, seemingly uninteresting results that go unnoticed can, years later, turn out to be **breakthroughs**
- Time can act as a cleanser of noise by confining to its dustbins all these **overhyped works** — some organizations even turn scientific production into a cheap spectator sport, with ranking of the "ten hottest papers" in sub-sub-specialties

### The Disease of Prizes for the Young

- If we replace scientific results with scientists, we often get the same **neomaniac hype** — there is a disease to grant a prize for a promising scientist "under forty," infecting economics, mathematics, finance, etc.
- Of the fields Taleb is familiar with (literature, finance, economics), he can pretty much ascertain that prizes given to those under forty are the **best reverse indicator of value** — much like the belief (well tested by traders) that companies hyped up as "best" on magazine covers or in books such as *Good to Great* are about to underperform (and one can derive abnormal profit by **shorting their stock**)
- The worst effect of these prizes is **penalizing those who don't get them** and debasing the field by turning it into an athletic competition

### If We Should Have a Prize

- If we should have a prize, it should be for **"over a hundred"**: it took close to one hundred and forty years to validate the contribution of **Jules Regnault**, who discovered optionality and mapped it mathematically — along with what was dubbed the philosopher's stone; his work stayed obscure all this time

### The Textbook Test

- To be convinced of how noisy science can be: take any elementary textbook you read in high school or college with interest — in any discipline — open it to a random chapter, and see if the idea is **still relevant**
- Odds are that it may be boring, but still relevant — or nonboring, and still relevant: it could be the **1215 Magna Carta** (British history), **Caesar's Gallic wars** (Roman history), a historical presentation of **the Stoics** (philosophy), an introduction to **quantum mechanics** (physics), or the **genetic trees of cats and dogs** (biology)
- Now try to get the proceedings of a random conference about the subject matter from **five years ago** — odds are it will feel no different from a five-year-old newspaper, perhaps even less interesting
- Attending breakthrough conferences is, statistically speaking, as much a waste of time as buying a **mediocre lottery ticket** — the odds of the paper being relevant and interesting in five years is no better than one in ten thousand: the **fragility of science!**

### Conversations with Academics vs. Amateurs

- Even the conversation of a **high school teacher** or that of an unsuccessful college professor is likely to be more worthwhile than the latest academic paper, less corrupted with neomania
- Taleb's best conversations in philosophy have been with **French lycée teachers** who love the topic but are not interested in pursuing a career writing papers (in France they teach philosophy in the last year of high school)
- **Amateurs** in any discipline are the best, if you can connect with them — unlike dilettantes, career professionals are to knowledge what prostitutes are to love
- Conversation with an academic is at best like conversation of plumbers, at worst **gossip about uninteresting people** (other academics), small talk
- The conversation of **top scientists** can sometimes be captivating — those people who aggregate knowledge and for whom cruising the subject is effortless as the entire small parts of the field come glued together — but these people are currently too rare on this planet

### Reading Rule: Favor the Old

- One of Taleb's students (majoring in economics) asked for a rule on what to read:

> "As little as feasible from the last twenty years, except history books that are not about the last fifty years."

- He blurted out this rule with irritation, hating such questions as "what's the best book you've ever read" or "what are the ten best books" — his "ten best books ever" list changes at the end of every summer
- He had been hyping Daniel Kahneman's recent book, because it is largely an exposition of his research of **thirty-five and forty years ago**, with filtering and modernization
- His recommendation seemed impractical, but after a while the student developed a culture in original texts such as **Adam Smith, Karl Marx, and Hayek** — texts he believes he will cite at the age of eighty
- After his detoxification, the student realized that all his peers do is read **timely material that becomes instantly obsolete**

### What Should Break

- In 2010, *The Economist* asked Taleb to imagine the world in 2036 — they wanted a contrarian "balance" to counter the numerous imaginative forecasts
- After a two-hour slow walk, he wrote a series of forecasts and sent them the text — they probably thought at first he was pulling a prank or someone got the wrong email
- Outlining the reasoning on **fragility and asymmetry** (concavity to errors), he explained he would expect the future to be populated with **wall-to-wall bookshelves**, the device called the telephone, artisans, and such — using the notion that most technologies twenty-five years old should be around in another twenty-five years (most, not all)
- The **fragile should disappear or be weakened**: the large, optimized, overreliant on technology, overreliant on the so-called scientific method instead of age-tested heuristics
- Corporations that are large today should be gone, as they have always been weakened by what they think is their strength: **size is the enemy of corporations** as it causes disproportionate fragility to Black Swans
- **City-states and small corporations** are more likely to be around, even thrive
- The **nation-state**, the currency-printing central bank, economics departments may stay nominally but will have their powers severely eroded
- What is in the left column of the Triad should be gone — alas to be replaced by other fragile items

### Prophets and the Present

- By issuing warnings based on **vulnerability** — that is, **subtractive prophecy** — we are closer to the original role of the prophet: to warn, not necessarily to predict, and to predict calamities *if people don't listen*
- The classical role of the prophet, at least in the **Levantine sense**, is not to look into the future but to talk about the present — he tells people what to do, or rather, the more robust what *not* to do
- In Near Eastern **monotheistic traditions** (Judaism, Christianity, Islam), the major role of prophets is the protection of monotheism from idolatrous and pagan enemies that may bring calamities on the straying population
- The prophet is someone in communication with the unique God, or at least can read his mind — and issues warnings to His subjects
- The Semitic *nby*, expressed as **Nevi** or **nebi** (original Hebrew), *nabi'y* (Aramaic), *nabi* (Arabic), is principally someone connecting with God, expressing what is on God's mind
- The meaning of *nab'* in Arabic is "news" (original Semitic root in Acadian, *nabu*, meant "to call")
- The initial Greek translation, **pro-phetes**, meant "spokesman," retained in Islam as a dual role for Mohammed the Prophet as the Messenger (*rasoul*)
- The job of mere **forecasting** is limited to seers or "astrologers" — dismissed by both the Koran and Old Testament
- The prophet deals only with the One God, not with the future like a mere Baalite

### The Persecution of Prophets

- Being a Levantine prophet has not been a particularly desirable professional occupation — acceptance was far from guaranteed
- Jesus mentioned the fate of **Elijah** (who warned against Baal, then ironically had to find solace in Sidon where Baal was worshipped) and announced that *no one becomes a prophet in his own land*
- The prophetic mission was not necessarily voluntary — consider **Jeremiah's** life, laden with *jeremiads* (lamentations), as his unpleasant warnings about destruction and captivity did not make him particularly popular
- Jeremiah was the personification of "shoot the messenger" and the expression **veritas odium parit** — truth brings hatred
- Jeremiah was beaten, punished, persecuted, and the victim of numerous plots involving his own brothers; apocryphal accounts even have him stoned to death in Egypt

### Greek Prophetic Tradition

- Further north of the Semites, in the **Greek tradition**, we find the same focus on messages, warnings about the present, and the same punishment inflicted on those able to understand things others don't
- **Cassandra** gets the gift of prophecy along with the curse of not being believed; when the temple snakes cleaned her ears she could hear some special messages
- **Tiresias** was made blind and transformed into a woman for revealing the secrets of the gods — but as consolation, Athena licked his ears so he could understand secrets in the songs of birds

### Lack of Recursive Thinking

- Recall the inability (from Chapter 2) to learn from past behavior — the problem with **lack of recursion in learning** (lack of second-order thinking)
- If those delivering messages deemed valuable for the long term have been persecuted in past history, one would expect a correcting mechanism — that intelligent people would learn from such historical experience so new messengers would be greeted with understanding — but **nothing of the sort takes place**
- This lack of recursive thinking applies not just to prophecy but to other human activities: if you believe a *new* idea will work and do well, you would expect people to pick up on it without too much reference to the perception of others — but they don't
- Something deemed "original" tends to be modeled on something that was new at the time but is no longer new — so being an Einstein for many scientists means solving a similar problem to the one Einstein solved, which was not a standard problem at all
- The very idea of being an Einstein in physics is **no longer original**
- People in **risk management** only consider risky things that have hurt them in the past (given their focus on "evidence"), not realizing that before these events took place, these occurrences were completely without precedent, escaping standards
- Taleb's personal efforts to make people step outside their shoes to consider these **second-order considerations** have failed — as have his efforts to make them aware of the notion of fragility

### Empedocles' Dog

- In Aristotle's *Magna Moralia*, there is a possibly apocryphal story about **Empedocles**, the pre-Socratic philosopher, who was asked why a dog prefers to always sleep on the same tile
- His answer was that there had to be some **likeness** between the dog and that tile (the story might be doubly apocryphal since we don't know if *Magna Moralia* was actually written by Aristotle himself)
- Consider the match between the dog and the tile: a **natural, biological, explainable or nonexplainable match**, confirmed by long series of recurrent frequentation — in place of rationalism, just consider the history of it
- Human technologies such as writing and reading that have survived are like the tile to the dog — a match between **natural friends**, because they correspond to something deep in our nature
- Every time someone tries to compare a book and an e-reader, or something ancient and a new technology, "opinions" pop up — as if reality cared about opinions and narratives
- There are secrets to our world that only **practice** can reveal, and no opinion or analysis will ever capture in full
- This secret property is revealed through **time**, and thankfully only through time

### What Does Not Make Sense

- If something that makes no sense to you (say, religion if you are an atheist, or some age-old habit or practice called irrational) has been around for a very, very long time, then irrational or not, you can expect it to **stick around much longer** and outlive those who call for its demise

## Chapter 21: Medicine, Convexity, and Opacity

> *What they call nonevidence — Where medicine fragilizes humans, then tries to save them — Newton's law or evidence?*

### Medicine as Dialectic Between Doing and Thinking

- The history of medicine is the story — largely documented — of the **dialectic between doing and thinking** and how to make decisions under opacity
- In the medieval Mediterranean, **Maimonides, Avicenna, Al-Ruhawi**, and the Syriac doctors such as **Hunain Ibn Ishaq** were at once philosophers and doctors
- A doctor in the medieval Semitic world was called **Al-Hakim** ("the wise"), or "practitioner of wisdom," a synonym for philosopher or rabbi (*hkm* is the Semitic root for "wisdom")
- Even in the earlier period there was a crop of Hellenized fellows who stood in the exact middle between medicine and philosophy — the great skeptic philosopher **Sextus Empiricus** was himself a doctor, a member of the skeptical empirical school
- So were **Menodotus of Nicomedia** and the experience-based predecessor of evidence-based medicine

### Via Negativa Decision Rule

- Simple, quite simple **decision rules and heuristics** emerge from this chapter
- **Via negativa** (by removal of the unnatural): only resort to medical techniques when the health payoff is very large (say, saving a life) and visibly exceeds its potential harm, such as incontrovertibly needed surgery or lifesaving medicine (penicillin)
- This is the same as with government intervention — squarely **Thalesian, not Aristotelian** (decision making based on payoffs, not knowledge)
- In these cases medicine has **positive asymmetries** — convexity effects — and the outcome will be less likely to produce fragility
- Otherwise, in situations where benefits of a particular medicine, procedure, or nutritional or lifestyle modification appear small — say, those aiming for comfort — we have a large potential **sucker problem** (on the wrong side of convexity effects)

### The Link Between Nonlinearity and Fragility in Medicine

- One of the unintended side benefits of the theorems Taleb and **Raphael Douady** developed in their paper mapping risk detection techniques (in Chapter 19) is an exact link between (a) **nonlinearity in exposure or dose-response** and (b) potential **fragility or antifragility**

### What Should Be Considered Evidence

- Taleb extends the problem to epistemological grounds and makes rules for *what should be considered evidence*: as with whether a cup should be considered half-empty or half-full, there are situations where we focus on **absence of evidence** and others where we focus on **evidence**
- In some cases one can be **confirmatory**, not others — it depends on the risks
- Take **smoking**: at some stage viewed as bringing small gains in pleasure and even health (people truly thought it was a good thing) — it took decades for its harm to become visible
- Had someone questioned it, they would have faced the canned-naive-academized response: **"do you have evidence that this is harmful?"** (the same type of response as "is there evidence that polluting is harmful?")
- The solution is simple, an extension of *via negativa* and Fat Tony's **don't-be-a-sucker rule**: the non-natural needs to prove its benefits, not the natural
- According to the statistical principle that nature is to be considered much less of a sucker than humans, in a complex domain, only **time — a long time — is evidence**

### The "Do You Have Evidence" Fallacy

- The **"do you have evidence" fallacy**: mistaking evidence of no harm for no evidence of harm, is similar to misinterpreting NED (no evidence of disease) for evidence of no disease
- This is the same error as mistaking **absence of evidence for evidence of absence** — one that tends to affect smart and educated people, as if education made people more confirmatory in their responses and more liable to fall into simple logical errors
- Under **nonlinearities**, the simple statements "harmful" or "beneficial" break down: **it is all in the dosage**

### How to Argue in an Emergency Room

- Taleb once broke his nose walking — for the sake of antifragility, walking on uneven surfaces as part of his antifragility program under the influence of **Loic Le Corre**, who believes in naturalistic exercise
- He felt the world was richer, more fractal, and contrasted this terrain with the smooth surfaces of sidewalks and corporate offices that felt like prisons
- In the emergency room, the doctor and staff insisted he should apply **"ice"** (ice-cold patch) to his nose — in the middle of the pain, it hit him that the swelling was most certainly not directly caused by the trauma but was his own **body's response to the injury**
- It seemed to him an insult to Mother Nature to override her programmed reactions unless we had good reason backed by proper empirical testing to show humans can do better — **the burden of evidence falls on us humans**
- He mumbled to the emergency room doctor whether he had any **statistical evidence** of benefits from applying ice to his nose or if it resulted from a naive version of *interventionism*
- The doctor's response: **"You have a nose the size of Cleveland and you are now interested in … numbers?"** — he had no answer
- Upon checking via computer, Taleb confirmed there is **no compelling empirical evidence** in favor of reduction of swelling (outside of very rare cases where swelling would threaten the patient)
- It was pure **sucker-rationalism** in the mind of doctors, coupled with interventionism — this need to *do something*, this defect of thinking and denigration of the unobserved
- This defect is not limited to swelling: this **confabulation plagues the entire history of medicine**
- Researchers **Paul Meehl and Robin Dawes** pioneered a tradition cataloging the tension between "clinical" and "actuarial" (statistical) knowledge, examining how many things believed true by professionals and clinicians aren't so and don't match empirical evidence
- The problem: these researchers did not have a clear idea of where the **burden of empirical evidence** lies (the difference between naive or pseudo empiricism and rigorous empiricism)
- The onus is on the doctors to show us why reducing fever is good, why eating breakfast before engaging in activity is healthy (there is no evidence), or why bleeding patients is the best alternative (they've stopped doing so)
- Sometimes the answer they give when they have no clue is: **"I am a doctor"** or **"are you a doctor?"**
- Taleb sometimes gets letters of support from alternative medicine fellows, which makes him go postal: the approach in this book is **ultra-orthodox, ultra-rigorous, and ultra-scientific**, certainly not in favor of alternative medicine

### Diseases of Civilization and Hidden Costs

- The **hidden costs of health care** are largely in the denial of antifragility
- It may not be just medicine — what we call **diseases of civilization** result from the attempt by humans to make life comfortable for ourselves against our own interest, since the comfortable is what fragilizes
- The rest of this chapter focuses on specific medical cases with hidden **negative convexity effects** (small gains, large losses) — and reframes the ideas of iatrogenics in connection with fragility and nonlinearities

### First Principle of Iatrogenics (Empiricism)

- The first principle of iatrogenics: we do not need **evidence of harm** to claim that a drug or an unnatural *via positiva* procedure is dangerous
- Recall the **turkey problem**: harm is in the future, not in the narrowly defined past — in other words, empiricism is not naive empiricism
- **Trans fat**: humans discovered how to make fat products; during the era of scientism they were convinced they could make it *better* than nature — not just equal, better
- Chemists assumed they could produce a fat replacement superior to lard or butter: synthetic products such as **margarine** stay soft in the refrigerator (more convenient), synthetic fats were derived from vegetables (more economical), and trans fat was assumed to be healthier
- Trans fat use was propagated very widely, and after a few hundred million years of consumption of animal fat, people suddenly got scared of "**saturated fat**" mainly from shoddy statistical interpretations
- Today trans fat is **widely banned** as it turned out it is behind heart disease and cardiovascular problems
- **Thalidomide**: a drug meant to reduce nausea episodes of pregnant women — it led to **birth defects**
