- **Diethylstilbestrol**: another drug that silently harmed the fetus and led to delayed **gynecological cancer** among daughters
- These two mistakes are telling because in both cases the benefits appeared to be **obvious and immediate, though small**, and the harm remained delayed for years — at least three-quarters of a generation

### Second Principle of Iatrogenics (Nonlinearity in Response)

- Second principle: iatrogenics is **not linear** — we should not take risks with near-healthy people but should take a lot, a lot more risks with those deemed in danger
- **Hypertension example**: when hypertension is mild ("normotensive"), the chance of benefiting from a certain drug is close to **5.6 percent** (only one person in eighteen benefits from treatment); when blood pressure is "high" or "severe," the chances of benefiting are **26 and 72 percent** respectively
- Treatment benefits are **convex to condition** — benefits rise disproportionally in an accelerated manner
- Iatrogenics should be constant for all categories — in the very ill condition, benefits are large relative to iatrogenics; in the borderline one, they are small
- We need to focus on **high-symptom conditions** and really ignore others where the patient is not very ill
- The argument is based on the structure of **conditional survival probabilities** — Mother Nature had to have tinkered through selection in inverse proportion to the rarity of the condition
- Of the hundred and twenty thousand drugs available today, one can hardly find a *via positiva* one that makes a healthy person unconditionally "better" — and if someone shows one, Taleb will be skeptical of yet-unseen side effects
- In a "mature" market there is **no free lunch** anymore; what appears as a free lunch has a hidden risk — in trading this was called a **"sucker's trade"**
- The more ill the person, the less likely nature would have found the solution by itself, in an **accelerating way** — a condition three units of deviation from the norm is more than three hundred times rarer than normal; five units of deviation is more than a million times rarer
- The medical community has **not modeled such nonlinearity** of benefits to iatrogenics — there is little explicit use of convexity biases in decision-making methodology
- Even risks seem to be **linearly extrapolated**, causing both underestimation and overestimation — a paper on radiation: "The standard model currently in use applies a linear scale, extrapolating cancer risk from high doses to low doses of ionizing radiation"
- **Pharmaceutical companies** are under financial pressure to find diseases and satisfy security analysts — scraping the bottom of the barrel, looking for disease among healthier and healthier people, lobbying for reclassifications of conditions, and fine-tuning sales tricks to get doctors to overprescribe
- Blood pressure in the upper range of "normal" is now reclassified as **"pre-hypertensive"** even with no symptoms — nothing wrong if it leads to healthier lifestyle and robust *via negativa* measures, but what's behind such classification is often a drive for more medication
- Taleb is not against pharma's function and mission but rather its business practice: they should focus *for their own benefit* on **extreme diseases**, not on reclassifications or pressuring doctors to prescribe
- The iatrogenics is in the patient, not in the treatment: if the patient is close to death, all speculative treatments should be encouraged — **no holds barred**; if the patient is near healthy, then **Mother Nature should be the doctor**

### Jensen's Inequality in Medicine

- The philosopher's stone: the **volatility of an exposure** can matter more than its average — the difference is the **"convexity bias"**
- If you are antifragile (convex) to a given substance, then you are better off having it **randomly distributed** rather than provided steadily
- Very few medical papers make use of nonlinearity by applying **convexity effects** to medical problems, in spite of the ubiquity of nonlinear responses in biology
- Taleb actually found only one explicit use of **Jensen's inequality** in one single application — thanks to his friend **Eric Briys** — and only one that used it properly
- The response "we know that" by medical researchers when the consequence of nonlinearity is explained to them is rather lame
- Convexity effects work in an identical way with **options, innovations**, anything convex — now applied to lungs
- People with lung diseases, including **acute respiratory distress syndrome**, used to be put on mechanical ventilators with constant pressure and volume — steadiness seemed a good idea
- But the reaction of the patient is **nonlinear to pressure** (convex over an initial range, then concave above it) and suffers from such regularity
- **J. F. Brewster** and associates figured out that dispensing higher pressure on occasion and low pressure at other times allowed them to provide a lot more volume to the lungs for a given mean pressure, thus **decreasing patient mortality**
- An additional benefit: an occasional spike in pressure helps to open up **collapsed alveoli** — that's how our lungs function when healthy, with variations and "noise" rather than steady airflow
- Humans are **antifragile to lung pressure** — this arises directly from the nonlinearity of the response since everything convex is antifragile up to a certain dosage
- People who deal with **nutrition** have not examined the difference between random calories and steady nutrition — something to return to in the next chapter
- Not using models of nonlinear effects such as convexity biases while "doing empirical work" is like having to catalog every apple falling from a tree and call the operation "empiricism" instead of just using **Newton's equation**

### Burying the Evidence

- What made medicine mislead people for so long is that its **successes were prominently displayed** and its mistakes literally buried — just like so many other interesting stories in the cemetery of history
- An illustration of intervention bias with **negative convexity effects**: in the 1940s and 1950s many children and teenagers received **radiation** for acne, thymus gland enlargement, tonsillitis, to remove birthmarks and treat ringworm of the scalp
- In addition to goiters and other late complications, approximately **7 percent** of patients who received this radiation developed thyroid cancer two to four decades later
- We should not write off radiation entirely — we are necessarily **antifragile to some dose of radiation** at naturally found levels; small doses may prevent injuries and cancers from larger ones, as the body develops some kind of immunity
- After hundreds of millions of years of having skin exposed to sun rays, we suddenly need so much protection from them — is it that our exposure is more harmful because of changes in the atmosphere, or populations mismatching the pigmentation of their skin to their environment, or that makers of **sun protection products** need to make profits?

### The Never-ending History of Turkey Situations

- The list of attempts to outsmart nature driven by **naive rationalism** is long — always meant to "improve" things, with continuous first-order learning (banning the offending drug or procedure but not figuring out that we could be making the mistake again elsewhere)
- **Statins**: meant to lower cholesterol; there is an asymmetry — one needs to treat **fifty high risk persons for five years** to avoid a single cardiovascular event; statins can potentially harm people who are not very sick, for whom the benefits are either minimal or totally nonexistent
- We will not be able to get an evidence-based picture of the **hidden harm** in the short term (remember smoking); arguments made in favor of routine administration of these drugs often lie in a few **statistical illusions or even manipulation**
- Drug companies seem to play on nonlinearities and **bundle the very ill and the less ill**, in addition to assuming that the metric "cholesterol" equates 100 percent with health
- Statins fail in applying the **first principle of iatrogenics** (unseen harm); further, they certainly *do* lower cholesterol, but your objective function as a human is not to lower a certain metric to pass a school-like test but to get in **better health**
- It is not certain whether these indicators people try to lower are **causes or manifestations** that correlate to a condition — just as muzzling a baby would prevent him from crying but would not remove the cause of his emotions
- **Metric-lowering drugs** are particularly vicious because of legal complexity: the doctor has the incentive to prescribe because should the patient have a heart attack, he would be sued for negligence; but the error in the opposite direction is not penalized at all, as side effects do not appear at all as being caused by medicine
- The same problem of **naive interpretation mixed with intervention bias** applies to cancer detection: there is a marked bias in favor of treatment, even when it brings more harm, because the legal system favors intervention
- **Surgery**: historians show that surgery had a much better track record than medicine for a long time — it was checked by the necessary rigor of visible results
- When operating on victims of very severe trauma (extracting a bullet, pushing bowels back in place), the iatrogenics is reduced; the downside of the operation is small compared to the benefits — hence **positive convexity effects**
- Unlike with usual pharmaceutical interventions, it is hard to say that Mother Nature would have done a better job — surgeons used to be **blue-collar workers**, or closer to artisans than high science, so they did not feel too obligated to theorize
- The two professions of medical doctor and surgeon were kept professionally and socially separate: one was an **ars** (craft built around experience-driven heuristics), the other **scientia** (reposed on theories, a general theory of humans)
- Surgeons were there for emergencies — in England, France, and some Italian cities, surgeons' guilds were merged with those of **barbers**
- The Soviet-Harvardification of surgery was for a long time constrained by the **visibility of the results** — you can't fool the eye
- Today's surgery, thanks to **anesthesia**, is done with a much smaller hurdle — surgeons now need to attend medical school (albeit a less theoretical one than the Sorbonne or Bologna of the Middle Ages)
- By contrast, in the past, **phlebotomy** (bloodletting) was one of the few operations performed by surgeons without any disincentive
- **Back surgery** done in modern times to correct sciatica is often useless, minus the possible harm from the operation — evidence shows that six years later, such an operation is on average equivalent to doing nothing, yet every operation brings risks (brain damage from anesthesia, medical error, exposure to hospital germs)
- Yet **spinal cord surgery** such as lumbar disc fusion is still practiced liberally, particularly as it is very lucrative for the doctor
- **Antibiotics**: every time you take an antibiotic, you help to some degree the mutation of germs into **antibiotic-resistant strains**; add to that the toying with your immune system — you transfer the antifragility from your body to the germ; the solution is to do it only when the benefits are large
- **Hygiene** (or excessive hygiene) has the same effect, particularly when people clean their hands with chemicals after every social exposure

### Verified and Potential Examples of Iatrogenics

- A list of verified and potential examples of iatrogenics (in terms of larger downside outside of very ill patients, whether such downside has been verified or not):
  - **Vioxx**: anti-inflammatory medicine with delayed heart problems
  - **Antidepressants** (used beyond the necessary cases)
  - **Bariatric surgery** (in place of starvation of overweight diabetic patients)
  - **Cortisone**
  - **Disinfectants/cleaning products** potentially giving rise to autoimmune diseases
  - **Hormone replacement therapy**
  - **Hysterectomies**
  - **Cesarean births** beyond the strictly necessary
  - **Ear tubes** in babies as an immediate response to ear infection
  - **Lobotomies**
  - **Iron supplementation**
  - **Whitening of rice and wheat** (once considered progress)
  - **Sunscreen creams** suspected to cause harm
  - **Hygiene** beyond a certain point (may make you fragile by denying **hormesis** — our own antifragility)
  - **Probiotics** (we ingest them because we don't "eat enough dirt" anymore)
  - **Lysol and other disinfectants** killing so many "germs" that kids' developing immune systems are robbed of necessary workout (or robbed of the "good" friendly germs and parasites)
  - **Dental** treatments

## Chapter 22: To Live Long, but Not Too Long

### Life Expectancy and Convexity
- Questioning medicine's "progress" prompts the sophistry that "we tend to live longer" — but this is the **exact same argument** as saying eating fresh food implies rejecting civilization, the rule of law, and humanism
- Life expectancy increased due to **sanitation, penicillin, drop in crime, life-saving surgery** — not routine medical treatment
- If we live longer because of medicine, it is thanks to **severe, lethal cases** (the convex cases) — not mild ailments where iatrogenics dominate
- **Hospital strikes** reveal that when only urgent cases are treated, life expectancy either increases or does not drop — a devastating indictment of routine medical intervention
- Many of the gains attributed to medicine are actually from **improvements in living conditions and social mores**

### Subtraction Adds to Your Life
- The **via negativa** approach to health: what you **don't do** matters more than what you do
- Remove **painkillers** (they mask root causes like sleep deprivation, tension, bad stressors), **vitamin pills**, **food supplements**, the family doctor, headache medicine
- **Oliver Wendell Holmes Sr.**: "if all the medications were dumped in the sea, it would be better for mankind but worse for the fishes"
- Taleb's father, an oncologist, raised him under the maxim that medications should be dumped in the sea — and followed it in practice
- Avoid fruits without ancient Greek or Hebrew names (mangoes, papayas, oranges are postmedieval candy equivalents)
- **Personal rule on liquids**: drink nothing that isn't at least a thousand years old — wine, water, coffee. No soft drinks. Orange juice is the most deceitfully noxious "healthy" drink
- What is called "healthy" is generally unhealthy, just as "social" networks are antisocial and the "knowledge" economy is typically ignorant
- Removing offensive irritants from life (morning newspapers, Thomas Friedman, Paul Krugman, the commute, air conditioning, television, gym "strength training" machines) produced a considerable jump in personal health

### The Iatrogenics of Money
- Construction laborers with ham and cheese baguettes seem **happier** than billionaires at Michelin restaurants — food tastes better after exertion
- Romans saw anything that "softens" or "mollifies" negatively — history likes the lurid; they understood comfort's side effects
- **Desert vs. city culture**: the desert is full of poetry, contemplation, frugality; the city breeds gossip, moral decay, decadence
- Christ fasted forty days in the Judean desert; Saint Simeon spent forty years on a column in Northern Syria — the tradition of shedding possessions
- Medical iatrogenics is the result of **wealth and sophistication**, not poverty — money has its own iatrogenics
- **The treadmill effect**: you need to make more and more to stay in the same place — greed is antifragile, though not its victims
- Wealth does not make people more independent — **we have never been richer in history, and never more in debt** (for the ancients, someone in debt was in bondage)
- **Tantalus class**: permanently tantalized, socialized into a certain milieu, exposed to a treadmill — you move to Greenwich, Connecticut, then become a pauper next to twenty-million-dollar mansions
- Romans circumvented treadmill effects through **patron-client relationships** — private charity, no welfare, no church
- Provincial landowners maintained "open house" with open tables — court life leads to corruption

### Religion and Naive Interventionism
- Religion has **invisible purposes** beyond what literal-minded scientistic-scientifiers identify — one purpose is to **protect us from scientism**
- For marginal disease, **anything that takes you away from the doctor and allows you to do nothing** will be beneficial — going to church (or the temple of Apollo) for mild cases helps
- The risk of iatrogenics exceeds the benefit of cure in cases with **negative convexity** — religion helps by limiting intervention
- Human nature "deep down, knows when to resort to the solace of religion, and when to switch to science"

### If It's Wednesday, I Must Be Vegan

- Taleb describes conference organizers asking about his dietary requirements — his answer depends on the day of the week because he follows the **Greek Orthodox calendar** and its required fasts
- This confuses the "Paleo camp" vs. "vegan camp" categorizers — the "Paleo" people try to replicate supposed ancestral high-meat, high-animal-fat diets; vegans eat no animal product at all
- It is a **naive rationalistic mistake** to be in either category permanently (except for religious or spiritual reasons) — episodic variation is the key
- Religion's heuristics accommodate rules while allowing occasional cheating — among other things, religion's role is to **tame the iatrogenics of abundance**: fasting makes you lose your sense of entitlement

### Convexity Effects and Random Nutrition

- **Jensen's inequality** applied to nutrition: irregularity has its benefits in some areas; regularity has its detriments — where Jensen's inequality applies, irregularity might be medicine
- What we mostly need to do is **remove a few meals at random**, or at least avoid steadiness in food consumption
- The error of missing **nonlinearities** is found in two places: in the mixture and in the frequency of food intake
- Humans are said to be **omnivorous** compared to specialized mammals like cows (salad-eaters) and lions (prey-eaters) — the ability to be omnivorous came in response to more variegated environments with unplanned, haphazard serial availability of sources
- **Specialization** is the response to a very stable habitat free of abrupt changes; **diversification of function** had to come in response to variety
- **Herbivores** are subjected to much less randomness than lions in food intake — they eat steadily but must work extremely hard to metabolize all nutrients, spending several hours a day eating
- The **lion** relies on more luck; it succeeds in a small percentage of kills (less than 20%), but when it eats, it gets all nutrients produced by the prey's hard work
- Principle derived from the random structure of the environment: **when we are herbivores, we eat steadily; when we are predators, we eat more randomly** — hence proteins need to be consumed randomly for statistical reasons
- If you agree that we need "balanced" nutrition of a certain combination, it is wrong to assume we need such balance **at every meal** rather than **serially** — there is a big difference between getting all nutrients together at every meal versus having them separately, serially
- **Deprivation is a stressor** — and we know what stressors do when allowed adequate recovery; **convexity effects** at work: getting three times the daily dose of protein in one day and nothing the next two is certainly not biologically equivalent to "steady" moderate consumption if our metabolic reactions are nonlinear
- Taleb is convinced (an inevitable result of nonlinearity) that **we are antifragile to randomness in food delivery and composition** — at least over a certain range, or number of days

### The Cretan/Mediterranean Diet Fallacy

- One blatant denial of **convexity bias** is the theory about the Cretan (or Mediterranean) diet that triggered a change in eating habits of the U.S. enlightened class, away from steak and potatoes in favor of grilled fish with salad and feta cheese
- What happened: someone looked at the **longevity of Cretans**, cataloged what they ate, then inferred naively that they lived longer because of the types of food consumed
- It could be true, but the **second-order effect** (the variations in intake) could be dominant — something that went unnoticed by mechanistic researchers
- The **Greek Orthodox church** has, depending on the severity of the local culture, almost **two hundred days of fasting per year** — and these are harrowing fasts
- Taleb describes writing during **Orthodox Lent**, a forty-day period in which almost no animal product can be consumed, no sweets, and for some sticklers, no olive oil
- He describes "ruse" foods from his ancestral village of **Amioun** in the Koura valley of Northern Lebanon — Levantine kibbeh made with herbs and beans instead of meat, meatballs made of matzoh-style small brown balls in a lentil soup
- The compensation for absent nutrients from daily diet will **take place in lumps** — deprivation of protein with fish on allowed days, then ravenously eating lamb on Easter Day, then consuming disproportionally high quantities of fatty red meat thereafter
- **Antifragility to the stressor of the fast**: it makes food taste better and can produce euphoria in one's system

> Breaking a fast feels like the exact opposite of a hangover.

### How to Eat Yourself

- People accept that the stressors of **exercise** are good for you, but do not transfer this to the point that **food deprivation** can have the same effect
- Scientists are in the process of discovering the effects of **episodic deprivation** of some, or all, foods — evidence shows we get sharper and fitter in response to the stress of the constraint
- Biological studies verify the existence of a human response to hunger: **biological mechanisms are activated by food deprivation**
- Experiments on cohorts show the positive effect of hunger — or deprivation of a food group — on the human body
- Researchers rationalize with the mechanism of **autophagy** (eating oneself): when deprived of external sources, cells start eating themselves, or breaking down proteins and recombining amino acids to provide material for building other cells
- It is assumed by some researchers that the **"vacuum cleaner" effect of autophagy** is the key to longevity — though Taleb notes his ideas are natural and impervious to their theories: occasional starvation produces some health benefits and that's that
- The response to hunger, our **antifragility**, has been underestimated — we've been telling people to eat a good meal for breakfast so they can face the day's travails, but this is not a new theory by empirically blind modern-day nutritionists
- In Stendhal's *Le rouge et le noir*, protagonist Julien Sorel is told "the work for the day will be long and rough, so let us **fortify** ourselves with a breakfast" (in the French of the period, breakfast was called "the first lunch")
- The idea of **breakfast as a main meal** with cereals and other such materials has been progressively shown to be harming humans — tests show that no benefits are derived from breakfast unless one has worked for it beforehand
- We are **not designed to be receiving food from the delivery person** — in nature, we had to expend energy to eat; lions hunt to eat, they don't eat their meal then hunt for pleasure; giving people food before they expend energy would confuse their signaling process
- Ample evidence that **intermittent food deprivation** has been shown to engender many beneficial effects on many functions
- **Valter Longo** noted that prisoners in concentration camps got less sick in the first phase of food restriction, then broke down later; he tried the result experimentally and found that mice, in the initial phases of starvation, can withstand high doses of chemotherapy without visible side effects
- Scientists use the narrative that starvation causes the expression of a gene coding a protein called **SIRT, SIRT1, or sirtuin**, which brings longevity and other effects
- The **antifragility of humans** manifests itself in the response with up-regulation of some genes in response to hunger

### Dose Response in Biology

- Religions with **ritual fasts** have more answers than assumed by those who look at them too literally — what these ritual fasts do is try to bring **nonlinearities in consumption** to match biological properties
- The Appendix shows graphically the standard **dose responses** in biology: a little bit of anything seems to harbor positive convexity effects (whether beneficial or harmful); add to it and the effect weakens
- Clearly at the upper end, the dose has **no additional effect** since one reaches saturation

### Walk-Deprived

- Another source of harm from **naive rationalism**: just as people tried to shorten their sleep for a long time, many people think that walking is useless, so they use mechanical transportation and get exercise at the gym
- When they walk, they do "power walks" sometimes with weights on their arms — they do not realize that **walking effortlessly, at a pace below the stress level**, can have some benefits
- Walking may be as necessary for humans as sleep — perhaps **as necessary as sleep**, which modernity at some point tried to reduce
- Since all ancestors until the advent of the automobile spent much of their time walking around (and sleeping), Taleb tries to just follow the logic, even before some medical journal catches up to the idea and produces what referees of medical journals call "evidence"

### I Want to Live Forever

- All people hear about is how to **live longer, richer**, and laden with electronic gadgets — we are not the first generation to believe the worst possible thing to befall us is death
- For the ancients, the worst possible outcome was not death, but **a dishonorable death**, or even just a regular one — for a classical hero, dying in a retirement home with tubes would not be the attractive *telos* for a life
- The modern illusion that we should **live as long as we can** — as if each individual were the end product — this idea of the "me" as a unit can be traced to the Enlightenment, and with it, **fragility**
- Before that, we were part of the present **collective and future progeny** — both present and future tribes exploited the fragility of individuals to strengthen themselves
- People engaged in **sacrifices, sought martyrdom**, died for the group, and derived pride from doing so; they worked hard for future generations
- Sadly, the modern economic system is loading future generations with **public governmental debt**, causing depletion of resources, and environmental blight to satisfy the requirements of the security analysts and the banking establishment — we cannot separate fragility from ethics
- The **gene is antifragile** since it is information, but the carrier of the gene is fragile, and needs to be so for the gene to get stronger — we live to produce information, or improve on it
- Nietzsche had the Latin pun *aut liberi, aut libri* — **either children or books**, both information that carries through the centuries
- Taleb was reading John Gray's *The Immortalization Commission* about attempts to use science in a postreligious world to achieve immortality — he felt deep disgust at the efforts of **"singularity" thinkers** (such as Ray Kurzweil) who believe in humans' potential to live forever
- If Taleb had to find the **anti-me**, the person with diametrically opposite ideas and lifestyle, it would be Ray Kurzweil — it is not just neomania; while Taleb proposes removing offensive elements from people's diets, Kurzweil works by adding, **popping close to two hundred pills daily**
- The **antifragility of a system** comes from the mortality of its components — Taleb sees himself as part of the larger population called humans; his purpose is to die a heroic death for the sake of the collective, to produce offspring, or eventually books — his genes, the antifragile in him, should be the ones seeking immortality, not him

> Then say goodbye, have a nice funeral in St. Sergius (Mar Sarkis) in Amioun, and, as the French say, *place aux autres*—make room for others.

### Footnotes on Chapter 22

- **Conditional life expectancy** numbers are revealing: Richard Lewontin estimates that in the last 50 years, only four months have been added to the expected life span of a person who is already 60 years old; CDC data shows life expectancy at age 20 only increased from 42.79 additional years in 1900–1902 to 51.2 in 1949–1951 and to 58.2 in 2002
- The **Cretan diet fallacy**: the second-order effect (the variations in intake via fasting) could be the dominant factor, not the specific foods consumed
- **Burden of evidence** on avoiding harm: a doctor who stopped eating sugar because of its potential harm was apologetic for doing so "without full evidence" — but the best test of empirical wisdom is in where one puts the burden of evidence
- Both sides of the fat vs. carbohydrates debate **ignore the nonlinearities** from sequence and composition — few realize we need to randomize protein intake
- The **principal disease of abundance**: habituation and jadedness (what biologists call dulling of receptors)

> Seneca: "To a sick person, honey tastes better."

---

## Book VII: The Ethics of Fragility and Antifragility

- Now, **ethics** — under opacity and in the newfound complexity of the world, people can hide risks and hurt others, with the law incapable of catching them
- **Iatrogenics** has both delayed and invisible consequences — it is hard to see causal links, to fully understand what's going on
- Under such epistemic limitations, **skin in the game** is the only true mitigator of fragility
- **Hammurabi's code** provided a simple solution close to thirty-seven hundred years ago — this solution has been increasingly abandoned in modern times, as we have developed a fondness for neomanic complication over archaic simplicity

---

## Chapter 23: Skin in the Game: Antifragility and Optionality at the Expense of Others

> *Making talk less cheap—Looking at the spoils—Corporations with random acts of pity?—Predict and inverse predict*

- This chapter examines what happens when **someone gets the upside, and a different person gets the downside**
- The worst problem of modernity lies in the **malignant transfer of fragility and antifragility** from one party to the other — one getting the benefits, the other (unwittingly) getting the harm, facilitated by the growing wedge between the ethical and the legal
- This is an **agency problem** — and the agency problem is, of course, an **asymmetry**

### The Disappearance of Heroism

- We are witnessing a fundamental change — consider older societies that have survived; the main difference between them and us is the **disappearance of a sense of heroism**: a shift away from respect and power to those who take downside risks for others
- **Heroism** is the exact inverse of the agency problem: someone elects to bear the disadvantage (risks his own life, or harm to himself, or accepts deprivation) for the sake of others
- What we have currently is the opposite: **power goes to bankers, corporate executives (nonentrepreneurs), and politicians**, who steal a free option from society
- Example of an inverse agency problem: the story of a **nanny who died to save a child** from being hit by a car — accepting death in someone else's place
- The word **"sacrifice"** is related to *sacred*, the domain of the holy that is separate from the profane

### Traditional Societies and the Value of Courage

- In traditional societies, a person is only as respectable as the **downside he or she is willing to face** for the sake of others
- The most courageous or valorous occupy the highest rank: **knights, generals, commanders** — even mafia dons accept that such rank makes them the most exposed
- The same applies to **saints**, who devote their lives to serve others — to help the weak, the deprived, and the dispossessed

### Table 7: Ethics and the Foundational Asymmetry (The Triad)

- **No Skin in the Game** (keeps upside, transfers downside to others, owns a hidden option at someone else's expense): Bureaucrats, Cheap talk ("tawk" in Fat Tony's lingo), Consultants/sophists, Businesses, Corporate executives (with suit), Theoreticians/data miners/observational studies, Centralized government, Editors, Journalists who "analyze" and predict, Politicians, Bankers, Fragilista Prof. Dr. Joseph Stiglitz, Risk vendors
- **Skin in the Game** (keeps his own downside, takes his own risk): Citizens, Actions (no tawk), Merchants/businessmen, Artisans, Entrepreneurs, Laboratory and field experimenters, Government of city-states, Writers, Speculators, Activists, Traders, Fat Tony
- **Skin in the Game for the Sake of Others, or Soul in the Game** (takes the downside on behalf of others, or universal values): Saints/knights/warriors/soldiers, Expensive talk, Prophets/philosophers (in the pre-modern sense), Artists/some artisans, Entrepreneurs/Innovators, Maverick scientists, Municipal government, Great writers, Journalists who take risks and *expose* frauds, Rebels/dissidents/revolutionaries, Nero Tulip, Taxpayers (not quite voluntarily soul in the game, but they are victims)

### Courage and Its Evolution

- The **robustness — even antifragility — of society** depends on heroes and people of courage; if we are here today, it is because someone, at some stage, took some risks for us
- Courage and heroism do not mean **blind risk taking** — it is not necessarily recklessness; there is a pseudocourage that comes from risk blindness, where people underestimate the odds of failure
- For the Stoics, **prudence is connatural to courage** — Publilius Syrus: prudence was deemed the courage of the general
- **Heroism has evolved** through civilization from the martial arena to that of ideas:
  - In **preclassical times**, the Homeric hero was principally endowed with physical courage
  - In **later classical times**, for the great Lacedaemonian king Agiselaus, a truly happy life was one crowned by the privilege of death in battle; courage had evolved from purely martial prowess into something greater — acts of **renunciation** and sacrifice for the collective
  - Finally, the **Socratic Plato** form of courage was born: the courage to stand up for an idea, and enjoy death in a state of thrill — the privilege of dying for truth or standing up for one's values had become the highest form of honor
- No one has had more prestige in history than **two thinkers who overtly and defiantly sacrificed their lives for their ideas** — two Eastern Mediterraneans; one Greek and one Semite

### Happiness and Middle Class Values

- We should pause when we hear **happiness** defined as an economic or materialistic condition
- Taleb is distraught by the glorified heroism-free **"middle class values"** that have spread via globalization: enshrining "hard work" for a bank or tobacco company, diligent newspaper reading, obedience, dependence on the opinion of a boss, reliance on stock market investments, tropical vacations, and a suburban life
- If success is random, a conscious act of **heroism is nonrandom** — the "ethical" middle class may work for a tobacco company and through casuistry call themselves ethical

### Half-Men and the Price of Dignity

- A **half-man** is not someone who does not have an opinion, just someone who does not take risks for it
- Historian **Paul Veyne** has shown that it is a big myth that gladiators were forced labor — most were volunteers who wanted the chance to become heroes by risking their lives
- Taleb's greatest lesson in courage came from his **father**: he had a large ego and demanded respect; when insulted by a militiaman at a road check during the Lebanese war, he refused to comply; as he drove away, the gunman shot him in the back; the bullet stayed in his chest for the rest of his life
- Dignity is worth **nothing unless you earn it**, unless you are willing to pay a price for it
- The notion of **megalopsychon** (a term expressed in Aristotle's ethics): a sense of grandeur that was superseded by the Christian value of "humility"; in Arabic it is called *Shhm* — best translated as **nonsmall**
- If you take risks and face your fate with dignity, there is nothing you can do that makes you small; if you don't take risks, there is nothing you can do that makes you grand

### Hammurabi

- We cannot separate **fragility and ethics** — just as only business school professors and similar fragilistas separate robustness and growth
- Some people have **options, or have optionality**, at the expense of others — and the others don't know it
- The effects of **transfers of fragility** are becoming more acute, as modernity builds up more people on the left column (inverse heroes): tenured government employees, academic researchers, journalists, the medical establishment, Big Pharma, and many more
- **Hammurabi's code** — now about 3,800 years old — identifies the need to reestablish a **symmetry of fragility**:

> If a builder builds a house and the house collapses and causes the death of the owner of the house—the builder shall be put to death. If it causes the death of the son of the owner of the house, a son of that builder shall be put to death. If it causes the death of a slave of the owner of the house—he shall give to the owner of the house a slave of equal value.

- They were **much more advanced 3,800 years ago** than we are today — the builder knows more about what lies hidden in the foundations than any safety inspector, making it the best risk management rule ever
- The foundation, with **delayed collapse**, is the best place to hide risk — Hammurabi and his advisors understood small probabilities
- The object is not to punish retrospectively, but to **save lives by providing up-front disincentive** in case of harm to others during the fulfillment of one's profession
- These asymmetries are particularly severe when it comes to **small-probability extreme events** (Black Swans) — as these are the most misunderstood and their exposure is easiest to hide

### Fat Tony's Two Heuristics

- First: **never get on a plane if the pilot is not on board**
- Second: **make sure there is also a copilot**
- The first heuristic addresses the asymmetry in rewards and punishment, or **transfer of fragility** between individuals
- **Ralph Nader's** rule: people voting for war need to have at least one descendant (child or grandchild) exposed to combat
- For the **Romans**, engineers needed to spend some time under the bridge they built — something that should be required of financial engineers today; the English went further and had the families of the engineers spend time with them under the bridge after it was built
- Every **opinion maker** needs to have "skin in the game" in the event of harm caused by reliance on his information or opinion — anyone producing a forecast or making an economic analysis needs to have something to lose from it
- **Forecasts induce risk taking** — they are more toxic than any other form of human pollution
- The second heuristic: we need to build **redundancy**, a margin of safety, avoiding optimization, mitigating (even removing) asymmetries in our sensitivity to risk

### The Talker's Free Option

- Society currently does the opposite of what it should: granting **mere talkers a free option** — we need to put entrepreneurs and risk takers at the top of the pyramid, and academizing academics, talkers, and political politicians at the bottom
- Fat Tony milking suckers when they ran to the exit door seemed at first inelegant to Nero — benefiting from the misfortune of others is not the most graceful approach to life
- But Tony had **something at risk** and would have been personally harmed by an adverse outcome — Fat Tony had no agency problem; this makes it permissible
- There is an even worse problem: people who just **talk, prognosticate, theorize** — no opinion without risk; no risk without hope for return

> If you have an opinion, you got to do so if you have an opinion. Otherwise, you do not really have an opinion at all.

- It is profoundly **unethical to talk without doing**, without exposure to harm, without having one's skin in the game — you express your opinion, it can hurt others who rely on it, yet you incur no liability
- This effect of **transferring fragility** is much more acute now, under modernity's connectivity and the newfound invisibility of causal chains — the intellectual today is vastly more powerful and dangerous than before; the "knowledge world" causes separation of knowing and doing (within the same person) and leads to the fragility of society

### Privilege and Obligation

- In the old days, **privilege came with obligations** — you want to be a feudal lord, you will be first to die; you want war, first in battle
- The U.S. Constitution: the **president is commander in chief** — Caesar, Alexander, and Hannibal were on the battlefield; the last, according to Livy, was first-in, last-out of combat zones
- George Washington went to battle, unlike Ronald Reagan and George W. Bush; even **Napoleon** was personally exposed to risks — his showing up during a battle was the equivalent of adding twenty-five thousand troops; Churchill showed an impressive amount of physical courage
- **Status implied you took physical risks** — in traditional societies even those who fail but have taken risks have a higher status than those who are not exposed
- The knowledge business means **shifting to talk** — talk by academics, consultants, and journalists, when it comes to predictions, can be just *talk*, devoid of embodiment and stripped of true evidence
- **Raymond Aron** was right in spite of his predictive abilities while those who were wrong survived beautifully; Aron was as colorless as they come while his enemy, Jean-Paul **Sartre**, got everything wrong yet looked radiant — the coward's books survived

### Thomas Friedman and the Problem of No Penalty

- Taleb describes seeing Thomas Friedman at Davos — the fragilista journalist who helped cause the Iraq war through his influential op-eds, paying no price for the mistake
- Ancient Mediterranean ethics: *Factum tacendo, crimen facias acrius* — for Publilius Syrus, **he who does not stop a crime is an accomplice**

> If you see fraud and don't say fraud, you are a fraud.

- Thomas Friedman was responsible for the Iraq invasion of 2003, and not only paid no penalty but continues to write for the op-ed page of *The New York Times*, confusing innocent people — he got and kept the upside, others get the downside
- His promotion of the **"earth is flat" idea of globalization** without realizing that globalization brings fragilities, causes more extreme events as a side effect, and requires a great deal of redundancies to operate properly
- **Natural and ancestral systems work by penalties**: no perpetual free option given to anyone — society in many things with visible effects works this way; if someone drives a school bus blindfolded and has an accident, he incurs enough penalties
- The problem is that Thomas Friedman is still driving the bus — there is **no penalty for opinion makers** who harm society; the Obama administration after the crisis of 2008 was populated with people who drove the bus blindfolded; the iatrogenists got promoted

### Postdicting

- Bureaucrats (whether in government or large corporations) live in a system of rewards based on narratives, "tawk," and the opinion of others, with job evaluation and peer reviews — in other words, what we call **marketing**
- This is Aristotelian: the biological world evolves by survival, not opinions and "I predicted" and "I told you so" — **evolution dislikes the confirmation fallacy**, endemic in society
- The economic world should also mess things up, but institutions may get bigger — **institutions block evolution** with bailouts and statism
- In the long term, social and economic evolution nastily takes place by surprises, discontinuities, and jumps

### Karl Popper's Missed Point

- Karl Popper's ideas on **evolutionary epistemology**: not being a decision maker, he was under the illusion that ideas compete with each other, with the least wrong surviving at any point in time
- He missed the point that it is not ideas that survive, but **people who have the right ones, or societies that have the correct heuristics**, or the ones, right or wrong, that lead them to do the good thing
- He missed the **Thalesian effect** — a wrong idea that is harmless can survive; those who have wrong heuristics but with a small harm in the event of error will survive
- **"Irrational" behavior can be good if it is harmless**
- Example of a **false belief helpful for survival**: which is more dangerous, to mistake a bear for a stone, or mistake a stone for a bear? It is hard for humans to make the first mistake — our intuitions make us overreact at the smallest probability of harm and fall for a certain class of false patterns
- Those who overreact upon seeing what may look like a bear have had a **survival advantage**; those who made the opposite mistake left the gene pool

> Our mission is to make talk less cheap.

### The Ancients and the Stiglitz Syndrome

- The ancients understood the **Stiglitz syndrome** — and associated ones — rather well, with quite sophisticated mechanisms to counter most aspects of **agency problems**, whether individual or collective (the circular effect of hiding behind the collective)
- The Romans forced engineers to **spend time under the bridge they built** — they would have had Stiglitz and Orszag sleep under the bridge of Fannie Mae and exit the gene pool

### Decimation and the Sacramentum

- Roman soldiers were forced to sign a **sacramentum** accepting punishment in the event of failure — a kind of pact between the soldier and the army spelling out commitment for upside and downside
- The **jungle problem**: facing a wild animal, two people can overcome it by joining forces, but each alone is weak — if you run away, all you need is to be faster than the other person, not the animal; the most optimal strategy would be to just be a coward and let the other perish
- The Romans removed the soldiers' incentive to be a coward through **decimation**: if a legion loses a battle and there is suspicion of cowardice, 10 percent of the soldiers and commanders are put to death, usually by random lottery
- The magic number is **one in ten** (or something equivalent): putting more than 10 percent to death would weaken the army; too little, and cowardice would be a dominant strategy
- The mechanism must have worked well as a deterrent against cowardice, since it was not commonly applied

### To Burn One's Vessels

- Playing on one's inner agency problem can go beyond symmetry: **give soldiers no options** and see how antifragile they can get
- On April 29, 711, the Arab commander **Tarek** crossed the Strait of Gibraltar from Morocco into Spain with a small army (the name Gibraltar is derived from the Arabic *Jabal Tarek*, meaning "mount of Tarek")
- Upon landing, Tarek had his **ships put to the fire**, then made a famous speech every schoolchild memorized:

> Behind you is the sea, before you, the enemy. You are vastly outnumbered. All you have is sword and courage.

- Tarek and his small army took control of Spain — the same heuristic has played out throughout history, from **Cortés in Mexico**, to **Agathocles of Syracuse** eight hundred years later
- Lesson: **never put your enemy's back to the wall**

### How Poetry Can Kill You

- The greatest Arabic poet is **Almutanabbi**, who lived about a thousand years ago — his poetry in the original has a hypnotic effect on the reader, rivaled only by Pushkin on Russian speakers
- Almutanabbi's name literally means "He who thinks of himself as a prophet" — his perceived oversized ego; he boasted that his poetry is so potent "that blind people can read it" and "deaf people can listen to it"
- Almutanabbi was a rare case of a **poet with skin in the game**, dying for his poetry — he had vilified a desert tribe in one of his poems and they were out to get him
- When outnumbered and fleeing, one of his companions started reciting his own boastful poem back at him — he turned around and confronted the tribe to his certain death
- Thus Almutanabbi remains, a thousand years later, the poet who died simply to **avoid the dishonor of running away** — and when we recite his verses we know they are genuine

### André Malraux — A Childhood Role Model

- **André Malraux** imbued his writings with his own risk taking — a school dropout, extremely well read, who became an adventurer in Asia in his twenties
- He was an active pilot during the Spanish Civil War and later an active member of the French underground resistance during the Second World War
- He turned out to be a bit of a mythomaniac, but unlike **Hemingway**, who was mostly into image building, Malraux was the real thing — he never engaged in small talk
- He supposedly said *the twenty-first century will be religious or will not be*

### The Problem of Insulation

- The philosopher **Myles Burnyeat** called this the "problem of insulation" — particularly with **skeptics who are skeptics in one domain but not another**
- Example: a philosopher who puzzles about the reality of time, but who nonetheless applies for a research grant to work on the philosophical problem of time during next year's sabbatical — without doubting the reality of next year's arrival
- The philosopher "insulates his ordinary first order judgments from the effects of his philosophizing"
- **Gerd Gigerenzer** reports a serious violation: **Harry Markowitz**, who started "portfolio selection" and received the Swedish Riskbank prize ("Nobel" in economics), does not use his own method for his portfolio — he has recourse to more sophisticated (and simpler to implement) cabdrivers' methodologies, closer to Mandelbrot's approach
- Heuristic: does the scientific researcher whose ideas are applicable to the real world **apply his ideas to his daily life?** If so, take him seriously. Otherwise, ignore him. (Exception: if doing pure mathematics, theology, or teaching poetry, there is no problem)

### Triffat-Type Fakeness: The Talker vs. the Doer

- Method of ignoring what an academic writes and **focusing on what he does** — Taleb met a researcher on happiness who held that *anything one makes beyond $50,000 does not bring any additional happiness*
- The researcher was earning more than twice that at a university, so according to his metric he was safe — yet a year later, Taleb heard that he was particularly avid for dollars and spent his time on the road speaking for fees
- That behavior was **more sufficient evidence than thousands of citations**

### Champagne Socialism

- The divorce between one's "tawk" and one's life can be overtly and convincingly visible — people who want others to live a certain way but don't really like it for themselves
- **Never listen to a leftist who does not give away his fortune** or does not live the exact lifestyle he wants others to follow
- The French call "the caviar left," *la gauche caviar*; Anglo-Saxons call them **champagne socialists** — people who advocate socialism while overtly leading a lavish lifestyle, often financed by inheritance
- Example: French president **François Mitterrand**, coming in on a socialist platform, emulated the pomp of French monarchs; his traditional archenemy, conservative **General de Gaulle**, led a life of old-style austerity
- Taleb's former client who tried to pressure him into writing a check for a candidate on a platform of higher taxes — heroic, since his own taxes would increase; but a year later, the client was being investigated for involvement in a scheme to be **shielded from taxes** while wanting *others* to pay more
- **Ralph Nader**: aside from astonishing personal courage and total indifference toward smear campaigns, he exhibits absolutely **no divorce between what he preaches and his lifestyle** — a secular saint

### Soul in the Game

- There is a class of people who escape bureaucrato-journalistic "tawk": those who have more than their skin in the game — *they have their soul in the game*
- Consider **prophets**: prophecy is a pledge of belief, little else; a prophet is not someone who first had an idea, but the one to first **believe in it** and take it to its conclusion
- Having skin in the game (and accepting downside) is what distinguishes the genuine thinker from ex post "tawk" — but there is one step beyond, to reach the rank of prophet: **doxastic commitment**
- **Doxastic commitment** (what philosophers call it): a type of belief-pledge that to Fat Tony and Nero needed to be translated into deeds (the reverse-Stiglitz); *Doxa* in Greek means "belief," distinguished from "knowledge" (episteme) — it involves a commitment of sorts beyond just words; in church Greek it took the meaning of *glorification*
- The main person behind a theory, the **originator**, is someone who believed in it in a doxastic way, with a costly commitment to take it to its natural conclusion — and not necessarily the first person to mention it over dessert wine or in a footnote
- Only he who has true beliefs will avoid eventually contradicting himself and falling into the errors of **postdicting**

## Chapter 23: Skin in the Game (cont.) — Options, Antifragility, and Social Fairness

### The Stock Market as Transfer of Antifragility

- The stock market: the greatest, industrial-sized, **transfer of antifragility** in history — due to a vicious form of asymmetric skin in the game
- The current system of packaging investments into shares of "public" corporations allowed managers to **game the system**, getting more prestige than the real risk takers, the entrepreneurs
- **The agency problem**: there is a difference between a manager running a company that is not his own and an owner-operated business in which the manager does not need to report numbers to anyone but himself, and has downside
- Corporate managers have **incentives without disincentives** — somehow these managers have been given free options by innocent savers and investors
- The U.S. stock market has cost retirees more than **three trillion dollars in losses** over the past dozen years compared to leaving money in government money market funds, while managers of the companies composing the stock market are richer by close to **four hundred billion dollars** thanks to the asymmetry of the stock option
- Even more outrageous: banks have **lost more than they ever made** in their history, with their managers being paid billions in compensation — taxpayers take the downside, bankers get the upside

### Transfer of Antifragility via Volatility

- The asymmetry is visibly present: **volatility benefits managers** since they only get one side of the payoffs — the more variations, the more value to this asymmetry; hence they are antifragile
- Two scenarios where the market does the same thing on average but follows different paths:
  - **Path 1**: market goes up 50 percent, then goes back down to erase all gains
  - **Path 2**: market does not move at all
- Path 1, the more volatile, is **more profitable to the managers** who can cash in their stock options — so the more jagged the route, the better it is for them
- Society — the retirees — has the **exact opposite payoff** since they finance bankers and chief executives; retirees get less upside than downside
- Society pays for the losses of the bankers, but gets **no bonuses from them** — if you don't see this transfer of antifragility as theft, you certainly have a problem
- The "incentive-based" system supposedly corresponds to capitalism, but managers' interests are aligned with shareholders only on the upside — **there is upside and no downside, no disincentive at all**

### The Robert Rubin Free Option

- **Robert Rubin**, former treasury secretary, earned $120 million from Citibank in bonuses over about a decade — the risks taken by the institution were hidden but the numbers looked good until they didn't (upon the turkey's surprise)
- Citibank collapsed, but he kept his money — taxpayers had to compensate him retrospectively since the government took over the banks' losses
- This type of payoff is very common: **thousands of other executives had it** — same as the architect hiding risks in the basement for delayed collapse and cashing big checks while protected by the legal system
- **Clawback provisions** suggested as a remedy (making people repay past bonuses in cases of subsequent failure) — but managers cannot cash their bonuses immediately, only after three to five years if there are no losses
- This does not solve the problem: **the managers still have a net upside and no net downside** — at no point is their own net worth endangered; the system still contains a high degree of optionality and transfer of fragility
- Same applies to fund managers involved in managing pension funds — they too have **no downside**

### Hammurabi's Rule and Historical Punishments

- Bankers used to be subjected to **Hammurabi's rule** — the tradition in Catalonia was to behead bankers in front of their own banks; bankers tended to skip town before failure was apparent (the fate of Francesco Castello, 1360)
- In modern times, only the **mafia** executes these types of strategies to remove the free option — in 1980, "Vatican banker" Roberto Calvi of Banco Ambrosiano that went bust, ran to London, supposedly committed suicide (later discovered the mafia killed him for losing their money)
- The same fate befell the Las Vegas pioneer **Bugsy Siegel**, who ran an unprofitable casino in which the mafia had investments
- In some countries such as Brazil, even today, **top bankers are made unconditionally liable** to the extent of their own assets

### Which Adam Smith?

- Many right-wingers who cite **Adam Smith** as patron saint of "capitalism" (a word he never uttered) do so without reading him, using his ideas in a self-serving selective manner
- In Book IV of *The Wealth of Nations*, Smith was extremely wary of giving someone **upside without downside** and had doubts about the limited liability of joint-stock companies (ancestor of the modern limited liability corporation)
- Smith detected the problem of managing **other people's business** — the lack of a pilot on the plane:

> The directors of such companies, however, being the managers rather of other people's money than of their own, it cannot well be expected, that they should watch over it with the same anxious vigilance with which the partners in a private copartnery frequently watch over their own.

- Smith was even suspicious of their economic performance: **"Joint-stock companies for foreign trade have seldom been able to maintain the competition against private adventurers."**
- The version of "capitalism" you need is with the **minimum number of people in the left column of the Triad** — the central problem of the Soviet system was that it put everyone in charge of economic life in that nasty fragilizing left column

## Chapter 23: Skin in the Game (cont.) — The Antifragility and Ethics of (Large) Corporations

### Corporations vs. Artisans

- While **corporations sell you junk drinks, artisans sell you cheese and wine** — there is a transfer of antifragility from the small in favor of the large, until the large goes bust
- The problem of the commercial world is that it only works by **addition (*via positiva*), not subtraction (*via negativa*)**: pharmaceutical companies don't gain if you avoid sugar; health club machine manufacturers don't benefit from you deciding to lift stones and walk on rocks
- All these firms have to produce "growth in revenues" to satisfy some semi-slow thinking MBA analyst sitting in New York — of course they will eventually self-destruct
- Companies like **Coke or Pepsi**: selling you sugary water or substitutes for sugar, putting into your body stuff that messes up your biological signaling system, *causing* diabetes and making diabetes vendors rich thanks to their compensatory drugs
- Large corporations can't make money selling you **tap water** and cannot produce wine (wine seems to be the best argument in favor of the artisanal economy)
- They dress their products up with a huge **marketing apparatus**, with images that fool the drinker — the arguments used against tobacco firms should apply to all other large companies that try to sell us things that may make us ill

### Debate with PepsiCo Chairperson

- Taleb and historian **Niall Ferguson** debated the chairperson of Pepsi-Cola at the New York Public Library — a great lesson in antifragility, as neither cared about who she was
- Both came totally unprepared while she showed up with a staff of aides with thick files — they could say anything with total impunity while she had to hew to her party line, lest the security analysts issue a bad report
- Corporate executives are **not entrepreneurs** — just slick actors (business schools are more like acting schools); someone intelligent or free would likely implode under such a regimen
- Niall detected her weak point: her slogan was that she contributed to employment by having six hundred thousand persons on her staff — he exposed her propaganda with the counterargument (developed by Marx and Engels) that large bureaucratic corporations seized control of the state just by being "big employers" and can extract benefits at the expense of small businesses

### Small Companies vs. Large Corporations

- A rule: with the exception of drug dealers, **small companies and artisans tend to sell us healthy products**, ones that seem naturally and spontaneously needed
- Larger ones — including pharmaceutical giants — are likely to be in the business of producing **wholesale iatrogenics**, taking our money, and then hijacking the state thanks to their army of lobbyists
- **Anything that requires marketing** appears to carry such side effects — you certainly need an advertising apparatus to convince people that Coke brings them "happiness" — and it works
- Exceptions exist: corporations with even the soul of artisans — Rohan Silva remarked that **Steve Jobs** wanted the inside of Apple products to look aesthetically appealing, although they are designed to remain unseen by the customer; this is something only a true artisan would do — carpenters with personal pride feel fake when treating the inside of cabinets differently from the outside

### Artisans, Marketing, and the Cheapest to Deliver

- There is **no product that Taleb particularly likes that he has discovered through advertising and marketing**: cheeses, wine, meats, eggs, tomatoes, basil leaves, apples, restaurants, barbers, art, books, hotels, shoes, shirts, eyeglasses, pants, olives, olive oil, etc.
- The same applies to cities, museums, art, novels, music, painting, sculpture — **word of mouth is a potent naturalistic filter**, actually the only filter
- The mechanism of **cheapest-to-deliver-for-a-given-specification** pervades whatever you see on the shelves — corporations have an incentive to provide you with the cheapest-to-produce piece of rubber containing the appropriate ingredients that can still be called cheese
- They are structurally designed and extremely expert at delivering the **cheapest possible product** that meets their specifications — same with business books: publishers and authors want to grab your attention and put in your hands the most perishable journalistic item that still can be called a book

### Marketing Is Bad Manners

- **Anything one needs to market heavily is necessarily either an inferior product or an evil one** — it is highly unethical to portray something in a more favorable light than it actually is
- Marketing is like a person on a boat cruise who starts boasting of his accomplishments — you would certainly run away or put him in contact with another talkative bore
- **Three layers of violations** by companies that advertise:
  - **First layer** (mild): companies are shamelessly self-promotional, like the man on the British Air flight — it only harms them
  - **Second layer** (more serious): companies trying to represent themselves in the most favorable light possible, hiding the defects of their products — still harmless, as we tend to expect it
  - **Third layer** (most serious): companies trying to **misrepresent the product** they sell by playing with our cognitive biases, our unconscious associations — showing a poetic picture of a sunset with a cowboy smoking and forcing an association between great romantic moments and a product that has no possible connection to it; you seek a romantic moment and what you get is cancer

### The Corporate System Pushes into the Third Layer

- At the core of the problem with capitalism: **a corporation does not have natural ethics**; it just obeys the balance sheet; its sole mission is satisfying some metric imposed by security analysts, themselves (very) prone to charlatanism
- A (publicly listed) corporation **does not feel shame**, does not feel pity, does not have a sense of honor (while marketing documents mention "pride"), does not have generosity — only self-serving actions are acceptable
- All of these defects are the result of the **absence of skin in the game** — an asymmetry that harms others for their benefit
- Such systems should tend to implode — but the problem of implosion does not matter to the managers because of the **agency problem**: their allegiance is to their own personal cash flow; they will not be harmed by subsequent failures; they will keep their bonuses
- In sum, corporations are so fragile, long-term, that they eventually collapse under the weight of the agency problem — while managers **milk them for bonuses and ditch the bones to taxpayers**
- They would collapse sooner if not for the lobby machines: they start **hijacking the state** to help them inject sugary drinks into your esophagus; in the United States large corporations control some members of Congress

### Lawrence of Arabia or Meyer Lansky

- If you ever have to choose between a mobster's promise and a civil servant's, **go with the mobster** — institutions do not have a sense of honor, individuals do
- During the Great War, **T. E. Lawrence** ("Lawrence of Arabia") struck a deal with Arab desert tribes to help the British against the Ottoman Empire — his promise was to deliver to them in return an Arab state
- But the French and British governments had made the secret **Sykes-Picot Agreement** to divide the area between themselves — Lawrence went back to live in the U.K. in frustration; lesson: **never trust the words of a man who is not free**
- A mobster's greatest asset is that **"his word is gold"** — a handshake from the famous mobster **Meyer Lansky** was worth more than the strongest contracts that a battery of lawyers could put together; he held in his mind the assets and liabilities of the Sicilian mafia without a single record, just his honor
- As a trader, Taleb never trusted transactions with "representatives" of institutions — pit traders are bound by their bonds, and he has never known a self-employed trader over a two-decade-long career who did not live up to his handshake

> Only a sense of honor can lead to commerce. Any commerce.

### Next (Chapter Summary)

- We saw how, thanks to the misunderstanding of antifragility (and asymmetry or convexity), some classes of people use **hidden options** and harm the collective without anyone realizing
- We also saw the solution in **forcing skin in the game**
- Next, we will look at another form of optionality: how people can **cherry-pick ethical rules** to fit their actions, or how they use public office as a means to satisfy personal greed

## Chapter 24: Fitting Ethics to a Profession

- *How the slaves can snatch control — Squeezing the sissies — The tantalized class, permanently tantalized*

### The Acute Modern Problem of Ethics and Profession

- At no time in the history of mankind has the following situation been seen in such an acute form: **Mr. John Smith Jr., JD**, is employed as a lobbyist for the tobacco industry in Washington, D.C., which is engaged in the business of **killing people for profit**
- The powers of subtraction: if we stopped such industries from existing (by, say, banning cigarettes), then everything else done by medicine becomes a footnote
- Ask any of his relatives why they can tolerate it — the answer is likely "everyone needs to make a living," as they are **hedging the possibility** of their falling into the same situation some day

### The Direction of the Arrow

- We need to test the direction of the arrow (using the same logic as lecturing birds on flying):
  - *Ethics (and Beliefs) → Profession* — or —
  - *Profession → Ethics (and Beliefs)*
- The question is: do people choose their profession based on their ethics, or do they **fit their ethics to their profession**?

### Fat Tony, Nero, and Socrates

- Prior to Fat Tony's debate with Socrates, Nero was curious about the first minute of encounter — what element of our physical environment would surprise Socrates the most after twenty-five centuries?
- Questioned by Fat Tony, Nero's speculative reply was: **"It would most certainly be the absence of slaves"**
- Fat Tony: "These people never did small domestic things themselves. So imagine Socrates' sorry figure of a bulging belly, spindly legs, wondering *Opou oi douloi?*" ("Where are the slaves?")

## Chapter 25: Conclusion

- At the end of the journey, **Shaiy Pilpel**, a probabilist with whom Taleb has had a decades-long calm conversation without small talk, asked him to explain the book standing on one leg
- Shaiy summed it up himself: all real ideas can be **distilled down to a central issue** that specialists completely miss — everything in religious law comes down to refinements of the Golden Rule: "Don't do unto others what you don't want them to do to you"
- The Golden Rule was a **true distillation**, not a Procrustean bed — a central argument is never a summary, it is more like a **generator**
- Shaiy's extraction: ***Everything gains or loses from volatility. Fragility is what loses from volatility and uncertainty.***
- The glass on the table is **short volatility** — it is dead; living things are **long volatility**

> "The best way to verify that you are alive is by checking if you like variations."

- **Prometheus is long disorder; Epimetheus is short disorder** — we can separate people by their exposure to and appetite for disorder
- Spartan hoplites contra bloggers, adventurers contra copy editors, Phoenician traders contra Latin grammarians, pirates contra tango instructors
- Everything nonlinear is **convex or concave, or both** — depending on stressor intensity. So everything likes or hates volatility **up to a point**
- We can detect what likes volatility thanks to **convexity** — we can build Black Swan-protected systems thanks to detection of concavity
- We can take **medical decisions** by understanding the convexity of harm and the logic of Mother Nature's tinkering
- Ethics is largely about **stolen convexities and optionality**
- More technically, we may never get to know *x*, but we can play with the exposure to *x*, barbell things to defang them — we can keep changing *f(x)* until comfortable, by a mechanism called **convex transformation** (the barbell)
- This short maxim tells you **where fragility supersedes truth**, why we lie to children, and why we got a bit ahead of ourselves in modernity
- **Distributed randomness** (as opposed to concentrated type) is a necessity, not an option — everything big is short volatility. Big and fast are abominations. Modern times don't like volatility.

> "The glass is dead; living things are long volatility. The best way to verify that you are alive is by checking if you like variations. Remember that food would not have a taste if it weren't for hunger; results are meaningless without effort, joy without sadness, convictions without uncertainty, and an ethical life isn't so when stripped of personal risks."

## Epilogue: From Resurrection to Resurrection

- **Fat Tony dies** of an aortic aneurism while Nero is in the Levant for his annual celebration of the death and rebirth of Adonis — a period of mourning with wailing women, followed by a celebration of resurrection
- Nero was designated executor of Tony's will — the estate was **flawless**: debt-free, conservative, fairly distributed, with discreet funds for a woman (antifragile obsessive love)
- **The posthumous prank**: Tony bequeathed Nero twenty million dollars to spend at his discretion on a **secret mission** — noble, but secret, vague, and dangerous
- It was the best compliment Nero ever got from Tony: **trusting that Nero would be able to read his mind**
- Which he did.

## Appendix I: Technical Companion (Continued)

### Technical Version of Fat Tony's "Not the Same 'Ting'" — The Conflation of Events and Exposure to Events

- This section also explains the **"convex transformation"**
- **f(x)** is *exposure* to the variable **x** — it can equivalently be called "payoff from x," "exposure to x," or even "utility of payoff from x," where we introduce **f** as a utility function; **x** can be anything
- **Example — Earthquake**: x is the intensity of an earthquake on some scale in some specific area; f(x) is the number of persons dying from it — we can easily see that f(x) can be made more predictable than x (if we force people to stay away from a specific area or build to some standards, etc.)
- **Example — Falling**: x is the number of meters of a fall to the ground when someone pushes you from height x; f(x) is a measure of physical condition from the effect of the fall — clearly you cannot predict x (who will push you), but you can manage f(x) (the effect)
- **Example — Traffic**: x is the number of cars in NYC at noon tomorrow; f(x) is travel time from point A to point B for a certain agent — f(x) can be made more predictable than x (take the subway, or even better, walk)
- Some people talk about f(x) thinking they are talking about x — this is the problem of the **conflation of event and exposure**
- This error is present in **Aristotle** and is virtually ubiquitous in the philosophy of probability (e.g., **Hacking**)
- One can become **antifragile to x without understanding x**, through convexity of f(x)
- The answer to "what do you do in a world you don't understand?" is simply: work on the undesirable states of f(x)
- It is often easier to modify f(x) than to get better knowledge of x — in other words, **robustification rather than forecasting Black Swans**
- **Example — Insurance barbell**: If you buy insurance on the market dropping more than 20%, f(x) will be independent of the part of the probability distribution of x that is below 20%, and impervious to changes in its scale parameter — this is an example of a **barbell**

### Convex Transformation (Figure 27)

- **Figure 27** illustrates the difference between x and exposure to x via a **convex transformation** (f(x) is a convex function of x)
- The top graph shows the **probability distribution of x** — a symmetrical bell curve
- The bottom graph shows the **probability distribution of f(x)** — right-skewed, with no downside risk
- The key is to modify f(x) in order to make knowledge of the properties of x on the left side of the distribution as irrelevant as possible
- This operation is called **convex transformation**, nicknamed **"barbell"**

### Green Lumber Fallacy (Technical Definition)

- **Green lumber fallacy**: When one confuses f(x) for another function g(x) — one that has different nonlinearities
- This is a precise technical restatement of why confusing the source of knowledge doesn't matter if you get the exposure function right

### Antifragility and Variance (Technical)

- If one is **antifragile to x**, then the variance (or other measures of variation) of x **benefit** f(x), since distributions that are skewed have their mean depend on the variance
- When skewed right, their expectation **increases with variance** — the lognormal, for instance, has for mean a term that includes **+½ σ²**
- The **probability distribution of f(x)** is markedly different from that of x, particularly in the presence of nonlinearities
- When f(x) is **convex (concave) monotonically**, f(x) is **right (left) skewed**
- When f(x) is increasing and convex on the left then concave to the right, the probability distribution of f(x) is **thinner-tailed** than that of x — for instance, in **Kahneman-Tversky's prospect theory**, the so-called utility of changes in wealth is more "robust" than that of wealth

### Why Payoff Matters More Than Probability (Technical)

- Where **p(x)** is the density, the expectation — ∫ f(x)p(x)dx — will depend increasingly on **f** rather than **p**
- The more **nonlinear f** is, the more the expectation depends on f rather than p
- This is a fundamental insight: the shape of the payoff function matters more than getting the probability distribution right

---

### The Fourth Quadrant (Taleb, 2009)

- The idea is that **tail events are not computable** (in fat-tailed domains), but we can assess our **exposure** to the problem
- Assume f(x) is an increasing function — **Table 10** connects the idea to the notion of the **Fourth Quadrant**:
  - **f(x) "mitigating" by clipping extreme outcomes (convex-concave)** + **Thin-tailed distribution for x** = Very robust outcome
  - **f(x) "mitigating" (convex-concave)** + **Fat-tailed distribution for x** = Quite robust outcome
  - **f(x) concave-convex, exacerbates remote outcomes** + **Thin-tailed distribution for x** = Robust outcome (sort of)
  - **f(x) concave-convex** + **Fat-tailed distribution for x** = **FOURTH QUADRANT** — Fragile (if f(x) is concave) or antifragile
- The **Fourth Quadrant** is the danger zone: fat-tailed distributions combined with concave-convex exposure functions that exacerbate remote outcomes

---

### Local and Global Convexities (Technical)

- **Nothing is open-ended in nature** — death is a maximum outcome for a unit
- Things end up **convex on one end, concave on the other**
- In fact, there is **maximum harm** at some point in things biological
- Revisiting the concave figure of the **stone and pebbles** from Chapter 18: by widening the range we see that **boundedness of harm brings convexities** somewhere
- **Concavity was dominant, but local** — Figure 28 shows the broader range
- **Figure 28** (top graph): At some point the concave turns convex as we hit **maximum harm** — the harm from the stone is bounded
- **Figure 28** (bottom graph): Shows strong antifragility with **no known upper limit** (leading to **Extremistan**) — these payoffs are only available in **economic variables** (sales of books, or matters unbounded or near-unbounded); Taleb is unable to find such an effect in nature

### Weak Antifragility (Mediocristan) — Figure 29

- **Figure 29** shows **weak antifragility** with a **bounded maximum** — typical in nature
- The response curve is S-shaped (sigmoid): initially convex, then concave, with a ceiling on maximum benefit
- This is the dose-response pattern seen in biological systems — **Mediocristan**-style antifragility

---

### Freak Nonlinearities (Very Technical)
